title="Battle of Issy">Issy</a></li></ul>\n<hr />\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Minor_campaigns_of_1815" title="Minor campaigns of 1815">Minor campaigns</a></b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rocheserviere" title="Battle of Rocheserviere">Rocheserviere</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Suffel" title="Battle of La Suffel">La Suffel</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Reduction_of_the_French_fortresses_in_1815" title="Reduction of the French fortresses in 1815">Reduction of the French fortresses</a></li></ul>\n<hr />\n<p><b>Caribbean</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Guadeloupe_(1815)" title="Invasion of Guadeloupe (1815)">Guadeloupe</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>The <b>Battle of Waterloo</b> was fought on Sunday, 18 June 1815, near <a href="/wiki/Waterloo,_Belgium" title="Waterloo, Belgium">Waterloo</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, then part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="United Kingdom of the Netherlands">United Kingdom of the Netherlands</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_North_(France)" title="Army of the North (France)">French army</a> under the command of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> was defeated by two of the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh Coalition">Seventh Coalition</a>: a <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British</a>-led Allied army under the command of the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> army under the command of <a href="/wiki/Gebhard_Leberecht_von_Bl%C3%BCcher" title="Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher">Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher, Prince of Wahlstatt</a>. The battle marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.\n</p><p>Upon Napoleon\'s return to power in March 1815, many states that had opposed him formed the Seventh Coalition, and began to mobilize armies. Wellington and Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s armies were <a href="/wiki/Canton_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton (military)">cantoned</a> close to the north-eastern border of France. Napoleon chose to attack them separately in the hope of destroying them before they could join in a co-ordinated invasion of France with other members of the coalition. On 16 June Napoleon successfully attacked the bulk of the Prussian army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ligny" title="Battle of Ligny">Battle of Ligny</a> with his main force, while at the same time a portion of the French army attacked an Allied army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Quatre_Bras" title="Battle of Quatre Bras">Battle of Quatre Bras</a>. Despite holding his ground at Quatre Bras, the defeat of the Prussians forced Wellington to withdraw north to Waterloo on the 17th. Napoleon sent a third of his forces to pursue the Prussians, who had <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign:_Ligny_through_Wavre_to_Waterloo" title="Waterloo Campaign: Ligny through Wavre to Waterloo">withdrawn parallel to Wellington</a> in good order. This resulted in the separate and simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wavre" title="Battle of Wavre">Battle of Wavre</a> with the Prussian rear-guard.\n</p><p>Upon learning that the Prussian army was able to support him, Wellington decided to offer battle on the <a href="/wiki/Mont-Saint-Jean,_Belgium" title="Mont-Saint-Jean, Belgium">Mont-Saint-Jean</a> <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">escarpment</a>, across the Brussels road. Here he withstood repeated attacks by the French throughout the afternoon of the 18th, aided by the progressively arriving Prussians. In the evening Napoleon committed his last reserves, the <a href="/wiki/French_Imperial_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="French Imperial Guard">French Imperial Guard</a>, to a desperate final attack, which was narrowly beaten back. With the Prussians breaking through on the French right flank, Wellington\'s Anglo-allied army counter-attacked in the centre, and the French army was routed.\n</p><p>Waterloo was the decisive engagement of the <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign" title="Waterloo Campaign">Waterloo Campaign</a> and Napoleon\'s last. According to Wellington, the battle was "the nearest-run thing you ever saw in your life".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon,_1815" title="Abdication of Napoleon, 1815">Napoleon abdicated</a> four days later, and on 7 July coalition forces entered Paris. The defeat at Waterloo ended Napoleon\'s rule as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor of the French</a>, and marked the end of his <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days" title="Hundred Days">Hundred Days</a> return from exile. This ended the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a>, and set a chronological milestone between serial European wars and decades of relative peace.\n</p><p>The battlefield is located in the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Braine-l%27Alleud" title="Braine-l&#39;Alleud">Braine-l\'Alleud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lasne" title="Lasne">Lasne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarcelis2015_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarcelis2015-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> about 15 kilometres (9.3&#160;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, and about 2 kilometres (1.2&#160;mi) from the town of <a href="/wiki/Waterloo,_Belgium" title="Waterloo, Belgium">Waterloo</a>. The site of the battlefield today is dominated by a large monument, the <a href="/wiki/Lion%27s_Mound" title="Lion&#39;s Mound">Lion\'s Mound</a>. As this mound was constructed from earth taken from the battlefield itself, the contemporary topography of the battlefield near the mound has not been preserved.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude">Prelude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prelude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign" title="Waterloo Campaign">Waterloo Campaign</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg/310px-Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg" width="310" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg/465px-Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg/620px-Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="975" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strategic_Situation_of_Western_Europe_1815.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The strategic situation in Western Europe in 1815: 250,000 Frenchmen faced a coalition of about 850,000 soldiers on four <a href="/wiki/Front_(military)" title="Front (military)">fronts</a>. Napoleon was forced to leave 20,000 men in Western France to reduce a royalist insurrection.</div></div></div>\n<p>On 13 March 1815, six days before Napoleon reached Paris, the powers at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Declaration_at_the_Congress_of_Vienna" class="extiw" title="s:Declaration at the Congress of Vienna">declared him an outlaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Brown_University_Library&#39;&#39;_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Brown_University_Library&#39;&#39;-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Four days later, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a> mobilised armies to defeat Napoleon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams199359_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams199359-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Critically outnumbered, Napoleon knew that once his attempts at dissuading one or more members of the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh Coalition">Seventh Coalition</a> from invading France had failed, his only chance of remaining in power was to attack before the coalition mobilised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChandler19661016,_1017,_1093_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler19661016,_1017,_1093-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Had Napoleon succeeded in destroying the existing coalition forces south of Brussels before they were reinforced, he might have been able to drive the British back to the sea and knock the Prussians out of the war. Crucially, this would have bought him time to recruit and train more men before turning his armies against the Austrians and Russians.\n</p><p>An additional consideration for Napoleon was that a French victory might cause French-speaking sympathisers in Belgium to launch a friendly revolution. Also, coalition troops in Belgium were largely second-line, as many units were of dubious quality and loyalty, and most of the British veterans of the <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a> had been sent to North America to fight in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChandler19661016,_1017,_1093_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler19661016,_1017,_1093-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The initial dispositions of British commander <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington</a>, were intended to counter the threat of Napoleon enveloping the Coalition armies by moving through <a href="/wiki/Mons" title="Mons">Mons</a> to the south-west of Brussels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne189582_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne189582-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> This would have pushed Wellington closer to the Prussian forces, led by <a href="/wiki/Gebhard_Leberecht_von_Bl%C3%BCcher" title="Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher">Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher</a>, but may have cut Wellington\'s communications with his base at <a href="/wiki/Ostend" title="Ostend">Ostend</a>. In order to delay Wellington\'s deployment, Napoleon spread false intelligence which suggested that Wellington\'s supply chain from the channel ports would be cut.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er2005136\xe2\x80\x93160_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er2005136\xe2\x80\x93160-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Napoleon_crop.jpg/170px-Napoleon_crop.jpg" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Napoleon_crop.jpg/255px-Napoleon_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Napoleon_crop.jpg/340px-Napoleon_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The resurgent <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon\'s</a> strategy was to isolate the Allied and Prussian armies and <a href="/wiki/Defeat_in_detail" title="Defeat in detail">annihilate each one separately</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg/170px-Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg" width="170" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg/255px-Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg/340px-Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg 2x" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Arthur_Wellesley_the_Duke_of_Wellington.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">1st Duke of Wellington</a>, commander of the Anglo-allied army</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bl%C3%BCcher_(nach_Gebauer).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg/170px-Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg/255px-Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg/340px-Bl%C3%BCcher_%28nach_Gebauer%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1006" data-file-height="1240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bl%C3%BCcher_(nach_Gebauer).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gebhard_Leberecht_von_Bl%C3%BCcher" title="Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher">Gebhard Leberecht von Bl\xc3\xbccher</a>, who had led one of the coalition armies defeating Napoleon at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig" title="Battle of Leipzig">Battle of Leipzig</a>, commanded the Prussian army</div></div></div>\n<p>By June, Napoleon had raised a total army strength of about 300,000 men. The force at his disposal at Waterloo was less than one third that size, but the rank and file were nearly all loyal and experienced soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerold196753,_58,_110_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerold196753,_58,_110-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon divided his army into a left wing commanded by <a href="/wiki/Michel_Ney" title="Michel Ney">Marshal Ney</a>, a right wing commanded by <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel,_marquis_de_Grouchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmanuel, marquis de Grouchy">Marshal Grouchy</a> and a reserve under his command (although all three elements remained close enough to support one another). Crossing the frontier near <a href="/wiki/Charleroi" title="Charleroi">Charleroi</a> before dawn on 15 June, the French <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign:_Start_of_hostilities_(15_June)" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterloo Campaign: Start of hostilities (15 June)">rapidly overran Coalition outposts</a>, securing Napoleon\'s "central position" between Wellington\'s and Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s armies. He hoped this would prevent them from combining, and he would be able to destroy first the Prussian\'s army, then Wellington\'s.\n</p><p>Only very late on the night of 15 June was Wellington certain that the Charleroi attack was the main French thrust. In the early hours of 16 June, at the <a href="/wiki/Duchess_of_Richmond%27s_ball" title="Duchess of Richmond&#39;s ball">Duchess of Richmond\'s ball</a> in Brussels, he received a dispatch from the <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_the_Netherlands" title="William II of the Netherlands">Prince of Orange</a> and was shocked by the speed of Napoleon\'s advance. He hastily ordered his army to concentrate on <a href="/wiki/Quatre_Bras" title="Quatre Bras">Quatre Bras</a>, where the Prince of Orange, with the brigade of <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Carl_of_Saxe-Weimar-Eisenach" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernhard Carl of Saxe-Weimar-Eisenach">Prince Bernhard of Saxe-Weimar</a>, was holding a tenuous position against the soldiers of Ney\'s left wing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971508_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971508-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ney\'s orders were to secure the crossroads of Quatre Bras, so that he could later swing east and reinforce Napoleon if necessary. Ney found the crossroads of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Quatre_Bras" title="Battle of Quatre Bras">Quatre Bras</a> lightly held by the Prince of Orange, who repelled Ney\'s initial attacks but was gradually driven back by overwhelming numbers of French troops. First reinforcements, and then Wellington arrived. He took command and drove Ney back, securing the crossroads by early evening, too late to send help to the Prussians, who had already been defeated.\n</p><p>Meanwhile on 16 June, Napoleon attacked and defeated Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s Prussians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ligny" title="Battle of Ligny">Battle of Ligny</a> using part of the reserve and the right wing of his army. The Prussian centre gave way under heavy French assaults, but the flanks held their ground. <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign:_Ligny_through_Wavre_to_Waterloo" title="Waterloo Campaign: Ligny through Wavre to Waterloo">The Prussian retreat from Ligny</a> went uninterrupted and seemingly unnoticed by the French. The bulk of their rearguard units held their positions until about midnight, and some elements did not move out until the following morning, ignored by the French.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesney1874144_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesney1874144-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crucially, the Prussians did not retreat to the east, along their own lines of communication. Instead, they, too, fell back northwards\xe2\x80\x94parallel to Wellington\'s line of march, still within supporting distance and in communication with him throughout. The Prussians rallied on <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Freiherr_von_B%C3%BClow" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Freiherr von B\xc3\xbclow">B\xc3\xbclow\'s</a> IV Corps, which had not been engaged at Ligny and was in a strong position south of <a href="/wiki/Wavre" title="Wavre">Wavre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesney1874144\xe2\x80\x93145_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesney1874144\xe2\x80\x93145-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the Prussian retreat from Ligny, Wellington\'s position at Quatre Bras was untenable. The next day he withdrew northwards, to a defensive position he had <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnoitred</a> the previous year\xe2\x80\x94the low <a href="/wiki/Ridge" title="Ridge">ridge</a> of Mont-Saint-Jean, south of the village of <a href="/wiki/Waterloo,_Belgium" title="Waterloo, Belgium">Waterloo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sonian_Forest" title="Sonian Forest">Sonian Forest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971527_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971527-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon, with the reserves, made a late start on 17 June and joined Ney at Quatre Bras at 13:00 to attack Wellington\'s army but found the position empty. The French pursued Wellington\'s retreating army to Waterloo; however, due to bad weather, mud and the head start that Napoleon\'s tardy advance had allowed Wellington, apart from a cavalry <a href="/wiki/Action_at_Genappe" class="mw-redirect" title="Action at Genappe">action at Genappe</a>, there was no substantial engagement.\n</p><p>Before leaving Ligny, Napoleon had ordered Grouchy, who commanded the right wing, to <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign:_Ligny_through_Wavre_to_Waterloo" title="Waterloo Campaign: Ligny through Wavre to Waterloo">follow up the retreating Prussians</a> with 33,000 men. A late start, uncertainty about the direction the Prussians had taken, and the vagueness of the orders given to him, meant that Grouchy was too late to prevent the Prussian army reaching Wavre, from where it could march to support Wellington. More importantly, the heavily outnumbered Prussian rear-guard was able to use the River Dyle to enable a savage and prolonged action to delay Grouchy.\n</p><p>As the 17th of June drew to a close, Wellington\'s army had arrived at its position at Waterloo, with the main body of Napoleon\'s army following. Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s army was gathering in and around Wavre, around 8 miles (13&#160;km) to the east of the city. Early on the morning of the 18th, Wellington received an assurance from Bl\xc3\xbccher that the Prussian army would support him. He decided to hold his ground and give battle.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Armies">Armies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Armies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Battle_of_the_Waterloo_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Battle of the Waterloo Campaign">Order of Battle of the Waterloo Campaign</a></div>\n<p>Three armies were involved in the battle: Napoleon\'s <i>Arm\xc3\xa9e du Nord</i>, a multinational army under Wellington, and a Prussian army under Bl\xc3\xbccher.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Marechal_Ney.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marechal_Ney.jpg/180px-Marechal_Ney.jpg" width="180" height="214" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marechal_Ney.jpg/270px-Marechal_Ney.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marechal_Ney.jpg/360px-Marechal_Ney.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2702" data-file-height="3217" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:YoungwilliamII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/YoungwilliamII.jpg/180px-YoungwilliamII.jpg" width="180" height="214" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/YoungwilliamII.jpg/270px-YoungwilliamII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/YoungwilliamII.jpg/360px-YoungwilliamII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2864" data-file-height="3400" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><i>Top,</i> Marshal <a href="/wiki/Michel_Ney" title="Michel Ney">Michel Ney</a>, who exercised tactical control of the greater part of the French forces for most of the battle. <i>Bottom</i>  <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_the_Netherlands" title="William II of the Netherlands">William, Prince of Orange</a>, commander of the Anglo-allied <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Battle_of_the_Waterloo_Campaign#I_Corps_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Battle of the Waterloo Campaign">I Corps</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The French army of around 69,000 consisted of 48,000 infantry, 14,000 cavalry, and 7,000 artillery with 250 guns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200575_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200575-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon had used conscription to fill the ranks of the French army throughout his rule, but he did not conscript men for the 1815 campaign. His troops were mainly veterans with considerable experience and a fierce devotion to their Emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlover201430_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlover201430-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The cavalry in particular was both numerous and formidable, and included fourteen regiments of armoured <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a>, and seven of highly versatile <a href="/wiki/Lancers" class="mw-redirect" title="Lancers">lancers</a> who were armed with lances, sabres and firearms.\n</p><p>However as the army took shape, French officers were allocated to units as they presented themselves for duty, so that many units were commanded by officers the soldiers didn\'t know, and often didn\'t trust. Crucially, some of these officers had little experience in working together as a unified force, so that support for other units was often not given.\n</p><p>The French army was forced to march through rain and black coal-dust mud to reach Waterloo, and then to contend with mud and rain as it slept in the open. Little food was available for the soldiers, but nevertheless the veteran French soldiers were fiercely loyal to Napoleon.\n</p><p>Wellington later said that he had "an infamous army, very weak and ill-equipped, and a very inexperienced Staff".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971485_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971485-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> His troops consisted of 67,000 men: 50,000 infantry, 11,000 cavalry, and 6,000 artillery with 150 guns. Of these, 25,000 were British, with another 6,000 from the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_German_Legion" title="King&#39;s German Legion">King\'s German Legion</a> (KGL). All of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> troops were regular soldiers, but only 7,000 of them were <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a> veterans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971484_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971484-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In addition, there were 17,000 Dutch and Belgian troops, 11,000 from <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Hanover</a>, 6,000 from <a href="/wiki/Brunswick-L%C3%BCneburg#Brunswick-Wolfenb\xc3\xbcttel" class="mw-redirect" title="Brunswick-L\xc3\xbcneburg">Brunswick</a>, and 3,000 from <a href="/wiki/Nassau_(duchy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nassau (duchy)">Nassau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200575\xe2\x80\x9376_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200575\xe2\x80\x9376-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many of the troops in the Coalition armies were inexperienced.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> The Dutch army had been re-established in 1815, following the earlier defeat of Napoleon. With the exception of the British and some from Hanover and Brunswick who had fought with the British army in Spain, many of the professional soldiers in the Coalition armies had spent some of their time in the French army or in armies allied to the Napoleonic regime. The historian Barbero states that in this heterogeneous army the difference between British and <i>foreign</i> troops did not prove significant under fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200619_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200619-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wellington was also acutely short of heavy cavalry, having only seven British and three Dutch regiments. The <a href="/wiki/Prince_Frederick,_Duke_of_York_and_Albany" title="Prince Frederick, Duke of York and Albany">Duke of York</a> imposed many of his staff officers on Wellington, including his second-in-command the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Paget,_1st_Marquess_of_Anglesey" title="Henry Paget, 1st Marquess of Anglesey">Earl of Uxbridge</a>. Uxbridge commanded the cavalry and had <a href="/wiki/Blank_cheque" title="Blank cheque">carte blanche</a> from Wellington to commit these forces at his discretion. Wellington stationed a further 17,000 troops at <a href="/wiki/Halle,_Belgium" title="Halle, Belgium">Halle</a>, 8 miles (13&#160;km) away to the west. They were mostly composed of Dutch troops under <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_the_Netherlands" title="William II of the Netherlands">Prince of Orange\'s</a> younger brother <a href="/wiki/Prince_Frederick_of_the_Netherlands" title="Prince Frederick of the Netherlands">Prince Frederick of the Netherlands</a>. They were placed as a guard against any possible wide flanking movement by the French forces, and also to act as a rearguard if Wellington was forced to retreat towards Antwerp and the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993239\xe2\x80\x93240_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993239\xe2\x80\x93240-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Prussian army was in the throes of reorganisation. In 1815, the former Reserve regiments, Legions, and <i>Freikorps</i> volunteer formations from the wars of 1813\xe2\x80\x931814 were in the process of being absorbed into the line, along with many <i><a href="/wiki/Landwehr" title="Landwehr">Landwehr</a></i> (militia) regiments. The <i>Landwehr</i> were mostly untrained and unequipped when they arrived in Belgium. The Prussian cavalry were in a similar state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200559_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200559-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Its artillery was also reorganising and did not give its best performance&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 guns and equipment continued to arrive during and after the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Offsetting these handicaps, the Prussian Army had excellent and professional leadership in its <a href="/wiki/German_General_Staff" title="German General Staff">General Staff</a> organisation. These officers came from four schools developed for this purpose and thus worked to a common standard of training. This system was in marked contrast to the conflicting, vague orders issued by the French army. This staff system ensured that before Ligny, three-quarters of the Prussian army concentrated for battle with 24 hours notice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Ligny, the Prussian army, although defeated, was able to realign its supply train, reorganise itself, and intervene decisively on the Waterloo battlefield within 48 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er200560\xe2\x80\x9362-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Two and a half Prussian army corps, or 48,000 men, were engaged at Waterloo; two brigades under B\xc3\xbclow, commander of IV Corps, attacked <a href="/wiki/Georges_Mouton" title="Georges Mouton">Lobau</a> at 16:30, while <a href="/wiki/Hans_Ernst_Karl_von_Zieten" class="mw-redirect" title="Hans Ernst Karl von Zieten">Zieten\'s</a> I Corps and parts of <a href="/wiki/Pirch_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Pirch I">Pirch I\'s</a> II Corps engaged at about 18:00.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battlefield">Battlefield</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Battlefield">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Waterloo_Battlefield_locations" title="List of Waterloo Battlefield locations">List of Waterloo Battlefield locations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_(14152126362).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg/310px-Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg" width="310" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg/465px-Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg/620px-Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_%2814152126362%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belgium-6773_-_Battlefield_View_(14152126362).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of the battlefield from the Lion\'s mound. On the top right are the buildings of <a href="/wiki/La_Haye_Sainte" title="La Haye Sainte">La Haye Sainte</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Waterloo position was a strong one. It consisted of a long ridge running east-west, perpendicular to, and bisected by, the main road to Brussels. Along the crest of the ridge ran the <a href="/wiki/Ohain,_Belgium" title="Ohain, Belgium">Ohain</a> road, a deep <a href="/wiki/Sunken_lane" title="Sunken lane">sunken lane</a>. Near the crossroads with the Brussels road was <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Elm" title="Waterloo Elm">a large elm tree</a> that was roughly in the centre of Wellington\'s position and served as his command post for much of the day. Wellington deployed his infantry in a line just behind the crest of the ridge following the Ohain road.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200578\xe2\x80\x9379_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200578\xe2\x80\x9379-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Using the <a href="/wiki/Reverse_slope_defence" title="Reverse slope defence">reverse slope</a>, as he had many times previously, Wellington concealed his strength from the French, with the exception of his <a href="/wiki/Skirmisher" title="Skirmisher">skirmishers</a> and artillery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200578\xe2\x80\x9379_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200578\xe2\x80\x9379-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The length of front of the battlefield was also relatively short at 2.5 miles (4.0&#160;km). This allowed Wellington to draw up his forces in depth, which he did in the centre and on the right, all the way towards the village of <a href="/wiki/Braine-l%27Alleud" title="Braine-l&#39;Alleud">Braine-l\'Alleud</a>, in the expectation that the Prussians would reinforce his left during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200580_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200580-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waterloo.1816.02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Waterloo.1816.02.jpg/220px-Waterloo.1816.02.jpg" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Waterloo.1816.02.jpg/330px-Waterloo.1816.02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Waterloo.1816.02.jpg/440px-Waterloo.1816.02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3218" data-file-height="4126" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waterloo.1816.02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1816 map showing the local geography, with Waterloo defending the approach to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In front of the ridge, there were three positions that could be fortified. On the extreme right were the ch\xc3\xa2teau, garden, and orchard of <a href="/wiki/Hougoumont_(farmhouse)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hougoumont (farmhouse)">Hougoumont</a>. This was a large and well-built country house, initially hidden in trees. The house faced north along a sunken, covered lane (usually described by the British as "the hollow-way") along which it could be supplied. On the extreme left was the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Papelotte" title="Papelotte">Papelotte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005149_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005149-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both Hougoumont and Papelotte were fortified and garrisoned, and thus anchored Wellington\'s <a href="/wiki/Flanking_maneuver" title="Flanking maneuver">flanks</a> securely. Papelotte also commanded the road to Wavre that the Prussians would use to send reinforcements to Wellington\'s position. On the western side of the main road, and in front of the rest of Wellington\'s line, was the farmhouse and orchard of <a href="/wiki/La_Haye_Sainte" title="La Haye Sainte">La Haye Sainte</a>, which was garrisoned with 400 light infantry of the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_German_Legion" title="King&#39;s German Legion">King\'s German Legion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005149_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005149-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> On the opposite side of the road was a disused sand quarry, where the <a href="/wiki/95th_Rifles" class="mw-redirect" title="95th Rifles">95th Rifles</a> were posted as sharpshooters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParry190058_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParry190058-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wellington\'s forces positioning presented a formidable challenge to any attacking force. Any attempt to turn Wellington\'s right would entail taking the entrenched Hougoumont position. Any attack on his right centre would mean the attackers would have to march between <a href="/wiki/Enfilade_and_defilade" title="Enfilade and defilade">enfilading fire</a> from Hougoumont and La Haye Sainte. On the left, any attack would also be enfiladed by fire from La Haye Sainte and its adjoining sandpit, and any attempt at turning the left flank would entail fighting through the lanes and hedgerows surrounding Papelotte and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Waterloo_Battlefield_locations#Eastern_end_of_the_line_of_battle" title="List of Waterloo Battlefield locations">other garrisoned buildings</a> on that flank, and some very wet ground in the Smohain <a href="/wiki/Defile_(geography)" title="Defile (geography)">defile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005141,_235_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005141,_235-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French army formed on the slopes of another ridge to the south. Napoleon could not see Wellington\'s positions, so he drew his forces up symmetrically about the Brussels road. On the right was I Corps under <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Drouet,_Comte_d%27Erlon" title="Jean-Baptiste Drouet, Comte d&#39;Erlon">d\'Erlon</a> with 16,000 infantry and 1,500 cavalry, plus a cavalry reserve of 4,700. On the left was II Corps under <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Charles_Reille" title="Honor\xc3\xa9 Charles Reille">Reille</a> with 13,000 infantry, and 1,300 cavalry, and a cavalry reserve of 4,600. In the centre about the road south of the inn <i><a href="/wiki/La_Belle_Alliance" title="La Belle Alliance">La Belle Alliance</a></i> were a reserve including Lobau\'s VI Corps with 6,000 men, the 13,000 infantry of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guard_(Napoleon_I)" title="Imperial Guard (Napoleon I)">Imperial Guard</a>, and a cavalry reserve of 2,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200583\xe2\x80\x9385_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200583\xe2\x80\x9385-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the right rear of the French position was the substantial village of <a href="/wiki/Plancenoit" title="Plancenoit">Plancenoit</a>, and at the extreme right, the <i>Bois de Paris</i> wood. Napoleon initially commanded the battle from Rossomme farm, where he could see the entire battlefield, but moved to a position near <i>La Belle Alliance</i> early in the afternoon. Command on the battlefield (which was largely hidden from his view) was delegated to Ney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200591_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200591-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:882px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg/880px-Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg" width="880" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg/1320px-Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg/1760px-Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6882" data-file-height="1078" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_waterloo_v2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A panorama of the Waterloo battlefield in 2012</div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle">Battle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Battle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg/310px-Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg.png" width="310" height="353" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg/465px-Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg/620px-Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waterloo_Campaign_map-alt3.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Waterloo campaign</div></div></div>\n<p>Wellington rose at around 02:00 or 03:00 on 18 June, and wrote letters until dawn. He had earlier written to Bl\xc3\xbccher confirming that he would give battle at Mont-Saint-Jean if Bl\xc3\xbccher could provide him with at least one corps; otherwise he would retreat towards Brussels. At a late-night council, Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s chief of staff, <a href="/wiki/August_Neidhardt_von_Gneisenau" title="August Neidhardt von Gneisenau">August Neidhardt von Gneisenau</a>, had been distrustful of Wellington\'s strategy, but Bl\xc3\xbccher persuaded him that they should march to join Wellington\'s army. In the morning Wellington duly received a reply from Bl\xc3\xbccher, promising to support him with three corps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971535\xe2\x80\x93536_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971535\xe2\x80\x93536-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 06:00 Wellington was in the field supervising the deployment of his forces. At Wavre, the Prussian IV Corps under B\xc3\xbclow was designated to lead the march to Waterloo as it was in the best shape, not having been involved in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ligny" title="Battle of Ligny">Battle of Ligny</a>. Although they had not taken casualties, IV Corps had been marching for two days, covering the retreat of the three other corps of the Prussian army from the battlefield of Ligny. They had been posted farthest away from the battlefield, and progress was very slow. The roads were in poor condition after the night\'s heavy rain, and B\xc3\xbclow\'s men had to pass through the congested streets of Wavre and move 88&#160;artillery pieces. Matters were not helped when a fire broke out in Wavre, blocking several streets along B\xc3\xbclow\'s intended route. As a result, the last part of the corps left at 10:00, six hours after the leading elements had moved out towards Waterloo. B\xc3\xbclow\'s men were followed to Waterloo first by I Corps and then by II Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005141_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005141-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon breakfasted off silver plate at <i>Le Caillou</i>, the house where he had spent the night. When <a href="/wiki/Jean-de-Dieu_Soult" title="Jean-de-Dieu Soult">Soult</a> suggested that Grouchy should be recalled to join the main force, Napoleon said, "Just because you have all been beaten by Wellington, you think he\'s a good general. I tell you Wellington is a bad general, the English are bad troops, and this affair is nothing more than eating breakfast".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971547_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971547-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon\'s seemingly dismissive remark may have been strategic, given his maxim "in war, morale is everything". He had acted similarly in the past, and on the morning of the battle of Waterloo may have been responding to the pessimism and objections of his chief of staff and senior generals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2001163\xe2\x80\x93166_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2001163\xe2\x80\x93166-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg/290px-Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg" width="290" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg/435px-Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg/580px-Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2154" data-file-height="1348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon.Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mont-Saint-Jean,_Belgium" title="Mont-Saint-Jean, Belgium">Battle of Mont-Saint-Jean</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Later on, being told by his brother, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte" title="J\xc3\xa9r\xc3\xb4me Bonaparte">Jerome</a>, of some gossip overheard by a waiter between British officers at lunch at the \'King of Spain\' inn in Genappe that the Prussians were to march over from Wavre, Napoleon declared that the Prussians would need at least two days to recover and would be dealt with by Grouchy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200573_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200573-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Surprisingly, Jerome\'s overheard gossip aside, the French commanders present at the pre-battle conference at <i>Le Caillou</i> had no information about the alarming proximity of the Prussians and did not suspect that Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s men would start erupting onto the field of battle in great numbers just five hours later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2001xxxii_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2001xxxii-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon had delayed the start of the battle owing to the sodden ground, which would have made manoeuvring cavalry and artillery difficult. In addition, many of his forces had <a href="/wiki/Bivouac_shelter" title="Bivouac shelter">bivouacked</a> well to the south of <i>La Belle Alliance</i>. At 10:00, in response to a dispatch he had received from Grouchy six hours earlier, he sent a reply telling Grouchy to "head for Wavre [to Grouchy\'s north] in order to draw near to us [to the west of Grouchy]" and then "push before him" the Prussians to arrive at Waterloo "as soon as possible".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971548_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971548-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At 11:00, Napoleon drafted his general order: Reille\'s Corps on the left and d\'Erlon\'s Corps to the right were to attack the village of Mont-Saint-Jean and keep abreast of one another. This order assumed Wellington\'s battle-line was in the village, rather than at the more forward position on the ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonaparte1869292\xe2\x80\x93293_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonaparte1869292\xe2\x80\x93293-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> To enable this, Jerome\'s division would make an initial attack on Hougoumont, which Napoleon expected would draw in Wellington\'s reserves,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher1994&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUS-QQxjHnn8CpgPA20_20&#93;_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher1994[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUS-QQxjHnn8CpgPA20_20]-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> since its loss would threaten his communications with the sea. A <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Battery" title="Grand Battery">grande batterie</a></i> of the reserve artillery of I, II, and VI Corps was to then bombard the centre of Wellington\'s position from about 13:00. D\'Erlon\'s corps would then attack Wellington\'s left, break through, and roll up his line from east to west. In his memoirs, Napoleon wrote that his intention was to separate Wellington\'s army from the Prussians and drive it back towards the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero200595\xe2\x80\x9398_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero200595\xe2\x80\x9398-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hougoumont">Hougoumont</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hougoumont">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hougoumont" title="Hougoumont">Hougoumont</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg/220px-Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg/330px-Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg/440px-Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3735" data-file-height="2770" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_of_Hougoumont_during_the_Battle_of_Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fighting at the Hougoumont farm</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg/220px-800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg/330px-800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg/440px-800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:800px-North_gate_Hougoumont.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gate on the north side assaulted by the <i>1st L\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa8re</i> who were led by <i><a href="/wiki/Sous-lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Sous-lieutenant">sous-lieutenant</a></i> Legros<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamar2000119_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamar2000119-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The historian Andrew Roberts notes that "It is a curious fact about the Battle of Waterloo that no one is absolutely certain when it actually began".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts200555_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200555-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Wellington recorded in his dispatches that at "about ten o\'clock [Napoleon] commenced a furious attack upon our post at Hougoumont".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellesley1815&#39;&#39;To_Earl_Bathurst._Waterloo,_19th_June_1815&#39;&#39;_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellesley1815&#39;&#39;To_Earl_Bathurst._Waterloo,_19th_June_1815&#39;&#39;-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Other sources state that the attack began around 11:30.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> The house and its immediate environs were defended by four light companies of <a href="/wiki/Foot_Guards" class="mw-redirect" title="Foot Guards">Guards</a>, and the wood and park by Hanoverian <i><a href="/wiki/J%C3%A4ger_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="J\xc3\xa4ger (military)">J\xc3\xa4ger</a></i> and the 1/2nd Nassau.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005113\xe2\x80\x93114_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005113\xe2\x80\x93114-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The initial attack by Bauduin\'s brigade emptied the wood and park, but was driven back by heavy British artillery fire, and cost Bauduin his life. As the British guns were distracted by a duel with French artillery, a second attack by Soye\'s brigade and what had been Bauduin\'s succeeded in reaching the north gate of the house. Sous-Lieutenant Legros, a French officer, broke the gate open with an axe, and some French troops managed to enter the courtyard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThiers1862215_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThiers1862215-57">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Coldstream_Guards" title="Coldstream Guards">Coldstream Guards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scots_Guards" title="Scots Guards">the Scots Guards</a> arrived to support the defence. There was a fierce melee, and the British managed to close the gate on the French troops streaming in. The Frenchmen trapped in the courtyard were all killed. Only a young drummer boy was spared.\n</p><p>Fighting continued around Hougoumont all afternoon. Its surroundings were heavily invested by French light infantry, and coordinated attacks were made against the troops behind Hougoumont. Wellington\'s army defended the house and the hollow way running north from it. In the afternoon, Napoleon personally ordered the house to be shelled to set it on fire,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> resulting in the destruction of all but the chapel. Du Plat\'s brigade of the King\'s German Legion was brought forward to defend the hollow way, which they had to do without senior officers. Eventually they were relieved by the <a href="/wiki/71st_(Highland)_Regiment_of_Foot" title="71st (Highland) Regiment of Foot">71st Highlanders</a>, a British infantry regiment. Adam\'s brigade was further reinforced by <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Halkett" title="Hugh Halkett">Hugh Halkett\'s</a> 3rd Hanoverian Brigade, and successfully repulsed further infantry and cavalry attacks sent by Reille. Hougoumont held out until the end of the battle.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I had occupied that post with a detachment from General Byng\'s brigade of Guards, which was in position in its rear; and it was some time under the command of Lieutenant-Colonel MacDonald, and afterwards of Colonel Home; and I am happy to add that it was maintained, throughout the day, with the utmost gallantry by these brave troops, notwithstanding the repeated efforts of large bodies of the enemy to obtain possession of it.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Wellington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth181510_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth181510-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When I reached Lloyd\'s abandoned guns, I stood near them for about a minute to contemplate the scene: it was grand beyond description. Hougoumont and its wood sent up a broad flame through the dark masses of smoke that overhung the field; beneath this cloud the French were indistinctly visible. Here a waving mass of long red feathers could be seen; there, gleams as from a sheet of steel showed that the cuirassiers were moving; 400&#160;cannon were belching forth fire and death on every side; the roaring and shouting were indistinguishably commixed\xe2\x80\x94together they gave me an idea of a labouring volcano. Bodies of infantry and cavalry were pouring down on us, and it was time to leave contemplation, so I moved towards our columns, which were standing up in square.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Major Macready, Light Division, 30th British Regiment, Halkett\'s brigade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreasy1877&#91;httpwwwstandinsefifteen15ahtm_Chapter_XV&#93;_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreasy1877[httpwwwstandinsefifteen15ahtm_Chapter_XV]-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The fighting at Hougoumont has often been characterised as a diversionary attack to draw in Wellington\'s reserves which escalated into an all-day battle and drew in French reserves instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongford1971552\xe2\x80\x93554_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongford1971552\xe2\x80\x93554-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In fact there is a good case to believe that both Napoleon and Wellington thought that holding Hougoumont was key to winning the battle. Hougoumont was a part of the battlefield that Napoleon could see clearly,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005298_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005298-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and he continued to direct resources towards it and its surroundings all afternoon (33&#160;battalions in all, 14,000&#160;troops). Similarly, though the house never contained a large number of troops, Wellington devoted 21&#160;battalions (12,000&#160;troops) over the course of the afternoon in keeping the hollow way open to allow fresh troops and ammunition to reach the buildings. He moved several artillery batteries from his hard-pressed centre to support Hougoumont,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005305,_306_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005305,_306-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and later stated that "the success of the battle turned upon closing the gates at Hougoumont".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts200557_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200557-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Grand_Battery_starts_its_bombardment">The Grand Battery starts its bombardment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Grand Battery starts its bombardment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Waterloo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Battle_of_Waterloo.svg/310px-Battle_of_Waterloo.svg.png" width="310" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Battle_of_Waterloo.svg/465px-Battle_of_Waterloo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Battle_of_Waterloo.svg/620px-Battle_of_Waterloo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Waterloo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the battle: Napoleon\'s units are in blue, Wellington\'s in red, Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s in grey.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 80 guns of Napoleon\'s <i>grande batterie</i> drew up in the centre. These opened fire at 11:50, according to <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Hill,_1st_Viscount_Hill" title="Rowland Hill, 1st Viscount Hill">Lord Hill</a> (commander of the Anglo-allied II Corps),<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> while other sources put the time between noon and 13:30.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005131_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005131-66">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The <i>grande batterie</i> was too far back to aim accurately, and the only other troops they could see were skirmishers of the regiments of Kempt and Pack, and <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_George_de_Perponcher_Sedlnitsky" title="Hendrik George de Perponcher Sedlnitsky">Perponcher\'s</a> 2nd Dutch division (the others were employing Wellington\'s characteristic "<a href="/wiki/Reverse_slope_defence" title="Reverse slope defence">reverse slope defence</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993286_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993286-67">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup>\nNevertheless, the bombardment caused a large number of casualties. Although some projectiles buried themselves in the soft soil, most found their marks on the reverse slope of the ridge. The bombardment forced the cavalry of the Union Brigade (in third line) to move to its left, as did the Scots Greys, to reduce their casualty rate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993287_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993287-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleon_spots_Prussians">Napoleon spots Prussians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Napoleon spots Prussians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At about 13:00, Napoleon saw the first columns of Prussians around the village of <a href="/wiki/Lasne-Chapelle-Saint-Lambert" class="mw-redirect" title="Lasne-Chapelle-Saint-Lambert">Lasne-Chapelle-Saint-Lambert</a>, four or five miles (six to eight kilometres) away from his right flank\xe2\x80\x94about three hours march for an army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005136_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005136-70">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon\'s reaction was to have Marshal Soult send a message to Grouchy telling him to come towards the battlefield and attack the arriving Prussians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005145_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005145-71">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Grouchy, however, had been executing Napoleon\'s previous orders to follow the Prussians "with your sword against his back" towards Wavre, and was by then too far away to reach Waterloo. Grouchy was advised by his subordinate, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Maurice_G%C3%A9rard" title="\xc3\x89tienne Maurice G\xc3\xa9rard">G\xc3\xa9rard</a>, to "march to the sound of the guns", but stuck to his orders and engaged the Prussian III Corps rear guard under the command of <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant-General" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant-General">Lieutenant-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">Baron</a> <a href="/wiki/Johann_von_Thielmann" title="Johann von Thielmann">Johann von Thielmann</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wavre" title="Battle of Wavre">Battle of Wavre</a>. Moreover, Soult\'s letter ordering Grouchy to move quickly to join Napoleon and attack B\xc3\xbclow would not actually reach Grouchy until after 20:00.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_French_infantry_attack">First French infantry attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First French infantry attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A little after 13:00, I Corps\' attack began in large <a href="/wiki/Column_(formation)" title="Column (formation)">columns</a>. Bernard Cornwell writes "[column] suggests an elongated formation with its narrow end aimed like a spear at the enemy line, while in truth it was much more like a brick advancing sideways and d\'Erlon\'s assault was made up of four such bricks, each one a division of French infantry".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECornwell2015&#39;&#39;Those_terrible_grey_horses,_how_they_fight&#39;&#39;_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECornwell2015&#39;&#39;Those_terrible_grey_horses,_how_they_fight&#39;&#39;-72">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Each division, with one exception, was drawn up in huge masses, consisting of the eight or nine battalions of which they were formed, deployed, and placed in a column one behind the other, with only five paces interval between the battalions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The one exception was the 1st Division (Commanded by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Joachim_J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Quiot_du_Passage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Joachim J\xc3\xa9r\xc3\xb4me Quiot du Passage (page does not exist)">Quiot</a>, the leader of the 1st Brigade).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Its two brigades were formed in a similar manner, but side by side instead of behind one another. This was done because, being on the left of the four divisions, it was ordered to send one (Quiot\'s brigade) against the south and west of La Haye Sainte, while the other (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Baron_Charles-Francois_Bourgeois&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Baron Charles-Francois Bourgeois (page does not exist)">Bourgeois\'</a>) was to attack the eastern side of the same post.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The divisions were to advance in <a href="/wiki/Echelon_(formation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Echelon (formation)">echelon</a> from the left at a distance of 400 paces apart \xe2\x80\x94 the 2nd Division (<a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Xavier_Donzelot" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois-Xavier Donzelot">Donzelot\'s</a>) on the right of Bourgeois\' brigade, the 3rd Division (<a href="/wiki/Pierre-Louis_Binet_de_Marcognet" title="Pierre-Louis Binet de Marcognet">Marcognet\'s</a>) next, and the 4th Division (<a href="/wiki/Pierre_Fran%C3%A7ois_Joseph_Durutte" title="Pierre Fran\xc3\xa7ois Joseph Durutte">Durutte\'s</a>) on the right.They were led by Ney to the assault, each column having a front of about a hundred and sixty to two hundred <a href="/wiki/File_(formation)" title="File (formation)">files</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaweis1908228-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The leftmost division, advanced on La Haye Sainte. The farmhouse was defended by the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_German_Legion" title="King&#39;s German Legion">King\'s German Legion</a>. While one French battalion engaged the defenders from the front, the following battalions fanned out to either side and, with the support of several squadrons of <a href="/wiki/Cuirassiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuirassiers">cuirassiers</a>, succeeded in isolating the farmhouse. The King\'s German Legion resolutely defended the farmhouse. Each time the French tried to scale the walls the outnumbered Germans somehow held them off. The Prince of Orange saw that La Haye Sainte had been cut off and tried to reinforce it by sending forward the Hanoverian L\xc3\xbcneberg Battalion in line. Cuirassiers concealed in a fold in the ground caught and destroyed it in minutes and then rode on past La Haye Sainte, almost to the crest of the ridge, where they covered d\'Erlon\'s left flank as his attack developed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993289\xe2\x80\x93293_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993289\xe2\x80\x93293-74">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At about 13:30, d\'Erlon started to advance his three other divisions, some 14,000&#160;men over a front of about 1,000 metres (1,100 yards), against Wellington\'s left wing. At the point they aimed for they faced 6,000&#160;men: the first line consisted of the Dutch 1st "<a href="/wiki/Brigade_van_Bylandt" title="Brigade van Bylandt">Brigade van Bylandt</a>" of the 2nd Dutch division, flanked by the British brigades of Kempt and Pack on either side. The second line consisted of British and Hanoverian troops under <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Picton" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Thomas Picton">Sir Thomas Picton</a>, who were lying down in dead ground behind the ridge. All had suffered badly at Quatre Bras. In addition, the Bijlandt brigade had been ordered to deploy its skirmishers in the hollow road and on the forward slope. The rest of the brigade was lying down just behind the road.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the moment these skirmishers were rejoining their parent battalions, the brigade was ordered to its feet and started to return fire. On the left of the brigade, where the 7th Dutch Militia stood, a "few files were shot down and an opening in the line thus occurred".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The battalion had no reserves and was unable to close the gap.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup> D\'Erlon\'s troops pushed through this gap in the line and the remaining battalions in the Bylandt brigade (8th Dutch Militia and Belgian 7th Line Battalion) were forced to retreat to the square of the 5th Dutch Militia, which was in reserve between Picton\'s troops, about 100 paces to the rear. There they regrouped under the command of Colonel <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Hendrik_van_Zuylen_van_Nijevelt" title="Pieter Hendrik van Zuylen van Nijevelt">Van Zuylen van Nijevelt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup> A moment later the Prince of Orange ordered a counterattack, which actually occurred around 10 minutes later. Bylandt was wounded and retired off the field, passing command of the brigade to Lt. Kol. De Jongh.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg/220px-Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg/330px-Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg/440px-Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="811" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrieux_-_La_bataille_de_Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Battle of Waterloo</i> by <a href="/wiki/Cl%C3%A9ment-Auguste_Andrieux" title="Cl\xc3\xa9ment-Auguste Andrieux">Cl\xc3\xa9ment-Auguste Andrieux</a></div></div></div>\n<p>D\'Erlon\'s men ascended the slope and advanced on the sunken road, <i>Chemin d\'Ohain</i>, that ran from behind La Haye Sainte and continued east. It was lined on both sides by thick hedges, with <a href="/wiki/Willem_Frederik_van_Bylandt" title="Willem Frederik van Bylandt">Bylandt\'s</a> brigade just across the road while the British brigades had been lying down some 100 yards back from the road, Pack\'s to Bylandt\'s left and Kempt\'s to Bylandt\'s right. Kempt\'s 1,900 men were engaged by Bourgeois\' brigade of 1,900 men of Quiot\'s division. In the centre, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Xavier_Donzelot" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois-Xavier Donzelot">Donzelot\'s</a> division had pushed back Bylandt\'s brigade. On the right of the French advance was Marcognet\'s division led by Grenier\'s brigade consisting of the <i>45e R\xc3\xa9giment de Ligne</i> and followed by the <i>25e R\xc3\xa9giment de Ligne</i>, somewhat less than 2,000 men, and behind them, Nogue\'s brigade of the <i>21e</i> and <i>45e</i> regiments. Opposing them on the other side of the road was <a href="/wiki/Denis_Pack" title="Denis Pack">Pack\'s</a> 9th Brigade consisting of three Scottish regiments: the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots" title="Royal Scots">Royal Scots</a>, the 42nd <a href="/wiki/Black_Watch" title="Black Watch">Black Watch</a>, the 92nd Gordons and the <a href="/wiki/44th_Foot" class="mw-redirect" title="44th Foot">44th Foot</a> totaling something over 2,000 men. A very even fight between British and French infantry was about to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006134\xe2\x80\x93138_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006134\xe2\x80\x93138-82">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French advance drove in the British skirmishers and reached the sunken road. As they did so, Pack\'s men stood up, formed into a four deep line formation for fear of the French cavalry, advanced, and opened fire. However, a firefight had been anticipated and the French infantry had accordingly advanced in more linear formation. Now, fully deployed into line, they returned fire and successfully pressed the British troops; although the attack faltered at the centre, the line in front of d\'Erlon\'s right started to crumble. Picton was killed shortly after ordering the counter-attack and the British and Hanoverian troops also began to give way under the pressure of numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993296\xe2\x80\x93297_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1993296\xe2\x80\x93297-83">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Pack\'s regiments, all four ranks deep, advanced to attack the French in the road but faltered and began to fire on the French instead of charging. The 42nd Black Watch halted at the hedge and the resulting fire-fight drove back the British 92nd Foot while the leading French <i>45e Ligne</i> burst through the hedge cheering. Along the sunken road, the French were forcing the Anglo-allies back, the British line was dispersing, and at two o\'clock in the afternoon Napoleon was winning the Battle of Waterloo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006138_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006138-84">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reports from <a href="/wiki/Karl_Freiherr_von_M%C3%BCffling" title="Karl Freiherr von M\xc3\xbcffling">Baron von Muffling</a>, the Prussian liaison officer attached to Wellington\'s army, relate that: "After 3 o\xe2\x80\x99clock the Duke\'s situation became critical, unless the succour of the Prussian army arrived soon".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002211_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002211-85">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_of_the_British_heavy_cavalry">Charge of the British heavy cavalry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Charge of the British heavy cavalry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Our officers of cavalry have acquired a trick of galloping at everything.
