They never consider the situation, never think of manoeuvring before an enemy, and never keep back or provide a reserve.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Wellington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142-86">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Forever!.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Scotland_Forever%21.jpg/310px-Scotland_Forever%21.jpg" width="310" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Scotland_Forever%21.jpg/465px-Scotland_Forever%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Scotland_Forever%21.jpg/620px-Scotland_Forever%21.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2995" data-file-height="1516" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Forever!.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Scotland Forever!</i>, the charge of the Scots Greys at Waterloo painted by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Thompson" title="Elizabeth Thompson">Elizabeth Thompson</a></div></div></div>\n<p>At this crucial juncture, Uxbridge ordered his two brigades of British heavy cavalry\xe2\x80\x94formed unseen behind the ridge\xe2\x80\x94to charge in support of the hard-pressed infantry. The <a href="/wiki/1st_Cavalry_Brigade_(United_Kingdom)" title="1st Cavalry Brigade (United Kingdom)">1st Brigade</a>, known as the Household Brigade, commanded by Major-General <a href="/wiki/Lord_Edward_Somerset" title="Lord Edward Somerset">Lord Edward Somerset</a>, consisted of guards regiments: the <a href="/wiki/1st_Regiment_of_Life_Guards" title="1st Regiment of Life Guards">1st</a> and <a href="/wiki/2nd_Regiment_of_Life_Guards" title="2nd Regiment of Life Guards">2nd Life Guards</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horse_Guards" title="Royal Horse Guards">Royal Horse Guards</a> (the Blues), and the <a href="/wiki/1st_King%27s_Dragoon_Guards" title="1st King&#39;s Dragoon Guards">1st (King\'s) Dragoon Guards</a>. The <a href="/wiki/2nd_Cavalry_Brigade_(United_Kingdom)" title="2nd Cavalry Brigade (United Kingdom)">2nd Brigade</a>, also known as the Union Brigade, commanded by Major-General <a href="/wiki/William_Ponsonby_(British_Army_officer)" title="William Ponsonby (British Army officer)">Sir William Ponsonby</a>, was so called as it consisted of an English, the <a href="/wiki/1st_The_Royal_Dragoons" title="1st The Royal Dragoons">1st (The Royals)</a>; a Scottish, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots_Greys" title="Royal Scots Greys">2nd (\'Scots Greys\')</a>; and an Irish, <a href="/wiki/6th_(Inniskilling)_Dragoons" title="6th (Inniskilling) Dragoons">6th (Inniskilling)</a>; regiment of heavy dragoons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001217_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001217-87">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990125_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990125-88">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kn%C3%B6tel_IV,_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg/170px-Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg" width="170" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg/255px-Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg/340px-Kn%C3%B6tel_IV%2C_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="653" data-file-height="945" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kn%C3%B6tel_IV,_04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Household Cavalry charging</div></div></div>\n<p>More than 20&#160;years of warfare had eroded the numbers of suitable cavalry mounts available on the European continent; this resulted in the British heavy cavalry entering the 1815 campaign with the finest horses of any contemporary cavalry arm. British cavalry troopers also received excellent mounted swordsmanship training. They were, however, inferior to the French in manoeuvring in large formations, cavalier in attitude, and unlike the infantry some units had scant experience of warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142-86">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Scots Greys, for example, had not been in action since 1795.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant197217_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant197217-89">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> According to Wellington, though they were superior individual horsemen, they were inflexible and lacked tactical ability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006140\xe2\x80\x93142-86">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> "I considered one squadron a match for two French, I didn\'t like to see four British opposed to four French: and as the numbers increased and order, of course, became more necessary I was the more unwilling to risk our men without having a superiority in numbers".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmanHall1902119_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmanHall1902119-90">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two brigades had a combined field strength of about 2,000 (2,651 official strength); they charged with the 47-year-old Uxbridge leading them and a very inadequate number of squadrons held in reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005188_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005188-91">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence that Uxbridge gave an order, the morning of the battle, to all cavalry brigade commanders to commit their commands on their own initiative, as direct orders from himself might not always be forthcoming, and to "support movements to their front".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlover2004&#39;&#39;Letter_16:_Frederick_Stovin_(ADC_to_Sir_Thomas_Picton)&#39;&#39;_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlover2004&#39;&#39;Letter_16:_Frederick_Stovin_(ADC_to_Sir_Thomas_Picton)&#39;&#39;-93">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> It appears that Uxbridge expected the brigades of <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Ormsby_Vandeleur" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Ormsby Vandeleur">Sir John Ormsby Vandeleur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hussey_Vivian,_1st_Baron_Vivian" title="Hussey Vivian, 1st Baron Vivian">Hussey Vivian</a> and the Dutch cavalry to provide support to the British heavies. Uxbridge later regretted leading the charge in person, saying "I committed a great mistake", when he should have been organising an adequate reserve to move forward in support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1891&#39;&#39;Letter_5&#39;&#39;,_pp._7\xe2\x80\x9310_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1891&#39;&#39;Letter_5&#39;&#39;,_pp._7\xe2\x80\x9310-94">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg/170px-Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg/255px-Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg/340px-Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2145" data-file-height="2794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Ansdell_%E2%80%94_The_Fight_For_The_Standard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Ewart" title="Charles Ewart">Sergeant Ewart</a> of the Scots Greys capturing the eagle of the <i>45\xc3\xa8me Ligne</i> in <i>The Fight For The Standard</i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ansdell" title="Richard Ansdell">Richard Ansdell</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Household Brigade crossed the crest of the Allied position and charged downhill. The <a href="/wiki/Cuirassiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuirassiers">cuirassiers</a> guarding d\'Erlon\'s left flank were still dispersed, and so were swept over the deeply sunken main road and then routed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005426,_note_18_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005426,_note_18-95">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;p&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The blows of the sabres on the cuirasses sounded like braziers at work.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Lord Edward Somerset.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoussaye1900182_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoussaye1900182-98">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Continuing their attack, the squadrons on the left of the Household Brigade then destroyed Aulard\'s brigade. Despite attempts to recall them, they continued past La Haye Sainte and found themselves at the bottom of the hill on blown horses facing Schmitz\'s brigade formed in <a href="/wiki/Infantry_square" title="Infantry square">squares</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2013160_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2013160-99">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To their left, the Union Brigade suddenly swept through the infantry lines, giving rise to the legend that some of the <a href="/wiki/92nd_(Gordon_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot" title="92nd (Gordon Highlanders) Regiment of Foot">92nd Gordon Highland Regiment</a> clung onto their stirrups and accompanied them into the charge.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;q&#93;</a></sup> From the centre leftwards, the Royal Dragoons destroyed Bourgeois\' brigade, capturing the eagle of the 105th <i>Ligne</i>. The Inniskillings routed the other brigade of Quoit\'s division, and the Scots Greys came upon the lead French regiment, 45th <i>Ligne</i>, as it was still reforming after having crossed the sunken road and broken through the hedge row in pursuit of the British infantry. The Greys captured the eagle of the 45th <i>Ligne</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005198\xe2\x80\x93204_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005198\xe2\x80\x93204-101">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and overwhelmed Grenier\'s brigade. These would be the only two <a href="/wiki/French_Imperial_Eagle" title="French Imperial Eagle">French eagles</a> captured by the British during the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006155_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006155-102">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> On Wellington\'s extreme left, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Fran%C3%A7ois_Joseph_Durutte" title="Pierre Fran\xc3\xa7ois Joseph Durutte">Durutte\'s</a> division had time to form squares and fend off groups of Greys.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg/170px-Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg/255px-Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg/340px-Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg 2x" data-file-width="820" data-file-height="1180" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_French_Lancer_by_Bellange.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Private of the Chevau-l\xc3\xa9gers of the line (lancers) who routed the Union Brigade</div></div></div>\n<p>As with the Household Cavalry, the officers of the Royals and Inniskillings found it very difficult to rein back their troops, who lost all cohesion. Having taken casualties, and still trying to reorder themselves, the Scots Greys and the rest of the Union Brigade found themselves before the main French lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994304_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994304-103">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Their horses were blown, and they were still in disorder without any idea of what their next collective objective was. Some attacked nearby gun batteries of the Grande Battery.\n<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWooten199342_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWooten199342-104">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Although the Greys had neither the time nor means to disable the cannon or carry them off, they put very many out of action as the gun crews were killed or fled the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005211_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005211-105">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Sergeant Major Dickinson of the Greys stated that his regiment was rallied before going on to attack the French artillery: Hamilton, the regimental commander, rather than holding them back cried out to his men "Charge, charge the guns!".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher1999252_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher1999252-106">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon promptly responded by ordering a counter-attack by the cuirassier brigades of Farine and Travers and Jaquinot\'s two <a href="/wiki/Chevau-l%C3%A9ger" title="Chevau-l\xc3\xa9ger">Chevau-l\xc3\xa9ger</a> (lancer) regiments in the I Corps <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a> division. Disorganized and milling about the bottom of the valley between Hougoumont and <a href="/wiki/La_Belle_Alliance" title="La Belle Alliance">La Belle Alliance</a>, the Scots Greys and the rest of the British heavy cavalry were taken by surprise by the countercharge of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Jean_Baptiste_Milhaud" title="\xc3\x89douard Jean Baptiste Milhaud">Milhaud\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Cuirassier" title="Cuirassier">cuirassiers</a>, joined by lancers from Baron Jaquinot\'s 1st Cavalry Division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994303\xe2\x80\x93304_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994303\xe2\x80\x93304-107">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As Ponsonby tried to rally his men against the French cuirassers, he was attacked by Jaquinot\'s lancers and captured. A nearby party of Scots Greys saw the capture and attempted to rescue their brigade commander. However, the French lancer who had captured Ponsonby killed him and then used his lance to kill three of the Scots Greys who had attempted the rescue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994304_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton-Williams1994304-103">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> By the time Ponsonby died, the momentum had entirely returned in favour of the French. Milhaud\'s and Jaquinot\'s cavalrymen drove the Union Brigade from the valley. The result was very heavy losses for the British cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895425\xe2\x80\x93426_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895425\xe2\x80\x93426-108">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er199986_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er199986-109">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> A countercharge, by British light dragoons under Major-General Vandeleur and Dutch\xe2\x80\x93Belgian light dragoons and <a href="/wiki/Hussar" title="Hussar">hussars</a> under Major-General <a href="/wiki/Charles_%C3%89tienne_de_Ghigny" title="Charles \xc3\x89tienne de Ghigny">Ghigny</a> on the left wing, and Dutch\xe2\x80\x93Belgian <i><a href="/wiki/Carabinier" title="Carabinier">carabiniers</a></i> under Major-General Trip in the centre, repelled the French cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005219\xe2\x80\x93223_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2005219\xe2\x80\x93223-110">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All figures quoted for the losses of the cavalry brigades as a result of this charge are estimates, as casualties were only noted down after the day of the battle and were for the battle as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;r&#93;</a></sup> Some historians, Barbero for example,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006142\xe2\x80\x93143_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006142\xe2\x80\x93143-113">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> believe the official rolls tend to overestimate the number of cavalrymen present in their squadrons on the field of battle and that the proportionate losses were, as a result, considerably higher than the numbers on paper might suggest.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;s&#93;</a></sup> The Union Brigade lost heavily in both officers and men killed (including its commander, William Ponsonby, and Colonel Hamilton of the Scots Greys) and wounded. The 2nd Life Guards and the King\'s Dragoon Guards of the Household Brigade also lost heavily (with Colonel Fuller, commander of the King\'s DG, killed). However, the 1st Life Guards, on the extreme right of the charge, and the Blues, who formed a reserve, had kept their cohesion and consequently suffered significantly fewer casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;t&#93;</a></sup> On the rolls the official, or paper strength, for both Brigades is given as 2,651 while Barbero and others estimate the actual strength at around 2,000<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006142\xe2\x80\x93143_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006142\xe2\x80\x93143-113">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;u&#93;</a></sup> and the official recorded losses for the two heavy cavalry brigades during the battle was 1,205 troopers and 1,303 horses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001217_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001217-87">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;v&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg/310px-De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg" width="310" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg/465px-De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg/620px-De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="9818" data-file-height="6500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:De_Slag_bij_Waterloo_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1115.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jan_Willem_Pieneman" title="Jan Willem Pieneman">Jan Willem Pieneman</a>\'s <i>The Battle of Waterloo</i> (1824). Duke of Wellington, centre, flanked on his left by Lord Uxbridge in hussar uniform. On the image\'s far left, Cpl. Styles of the Royal Dragoons flourishes the eagle of the 105<i>eme</i> <i>Ligne</i>. The wounded Prince of Orange is carried from the field in the foreground.</div></div></div>\n<p>Some historians, such as Chandler and Weller, assert that the British heavy cavalry were destroyed as a viable force following their first, epic charge. Barbero states that the Scots Greys were practically wiped out and that the other two regiments of the Union Brigade suffered comparable losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006164_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006164-119">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Other historians, such as Clark-Kennedy and Wood, citing British eyewitness accounts, describe the continuing role of the heavy cavalry after their charge. The heavy brigades, far from being ineffective, continued to provide valuable services. They countercharged French cavalry numerous times (both brigades),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1891Letters:_18,_26,_104_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1891Letters:_18,_26,_104-120">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111-121">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher2001142\xe2\x80\x93143_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher2001142\xe2\x80\x93143-122">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1895164,_171_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1895164,_171-123">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> halted a combined cavalry and infantry attack (Household Brigade only),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne189138_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne189138-124">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990144_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990144-125">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECotton184990\xe2\x80\x9391_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECotton184990\xe2\x80\x9391-126">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> were used to bolster the morale of those units in their vicinity at times of crisis, and filled gaps in the Anglo-allied line caused by high casualties in infantry formations (both brigades).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1891Letters_9,_18,_36_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1891Letters_9,_18,_36-127">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990146_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglesey1990146-128">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975110\xe2\x80\x93111_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975110\xe2\x80\x93111-129">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1895177_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1895177-130">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher1999270-271_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher1999270-271-131">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> This service was rendered at a very high cost, as close combat with French cavalry, carbine fire, infantry musketry and\xe2\x80\x94more deadly than all of these\xe2\x80\x94artillery fire steadily eroded the number of effectives in the two brigades.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;w&#93;</a></sup> At 6 o\'clock in the afternoon the whole Union Brigade could field only 3 squadrons, though these countercharged French cavalry, losing half their number in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111-121">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the fighting the two brigades, by this time combined, could muster one squadron.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne189139_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne189139-133">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark-Kennedy1975111-121">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1895177_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1895177-130">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>14,000 French troops of D\'Erlon\'s I Corps had been committed to this attack. The I Corps had been driven in rout back across the valley costing Napoleon 3,000 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEspositoElting1999354,_Map_166_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEspositoElting1999354,_Map_166-134">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> including over 2,000&#160;prisoners taken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006156_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006156-135">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Also some valuable time was lost, the charge had dispersed numerous units and it would take until 16:00 hours for D\'Erlon\'s shaken corps to reform. And although elements of the Prussians now began to appear on the field to his right, Napoleon had already ordered Lobau\'s VI corps to move to the right flank to hold them back before D\'Erlon\'s attack began.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_French_cavalry_attack">The French cavalry attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The French cavalry attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg/220px-Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg/330px-Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg/440px-Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3088" data-file-height="2056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marechal_Ney_%C3%A0_Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michel_Ney" title="Michel Ney">Marshal Ney</a> leading the French cavalry charge, from <a href="/wiki/Louis_Dumoulin" title="Louis Dumoulin">Louis Dumoulin\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Panorama_of_the_Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Panorama of the Battle of Waterloo">Panorama of the Battle of Waterloo</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>A little before 16:00, Ney noted an apparent exodus from Wellington\'s centre. He mistook the movement of casualties to the rear for the beginnings of a retreat, and sought to exploit it. Following the defeat of d\'Erlon\'s Corps, Ney had few infantry reserves left, as most of the infantry had been committed either to the futile Hougoumont attack or to the defence of the French right. Ney therefore tried to break Wellington\'s centre with cavalry alone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895443\xe2\x80\x93449_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895443\xe2\x80\x93449-136">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\nInitially <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Jean_Baptiste_Milhaud" title="\xc3\x89douard Jean Baptiste Milhaud">Milhaud\'s</a> reserve cavalry corps of cuirassiers and <a href="/wiki/Charles,_comte_Lefebvre-Desnouettes" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles, comte Lefebvre-Desnouettes">Lefebvre-Desno\xc3\xabttes\'</a> light cavalry division of the Imperial Guard, some 4,800&#160;sabres, were committed. When these were repulsed, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_%C3%89tienne_de_Kellermann" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois \xc3\x89tienne de Kellermann">Kellermann\'s</a> heavy cavalry corps and <a href="/wiki/Claude-%C3%89tienne_Guyot" title="Claude-\xc3\x89tienne Guyot">Guyot\'s</a> heavy cavalry of the Guard were added to the massed assault, a total of around 9,000&#160;cavalry in 67&#160;squadrons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001356_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001356-137">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> When Napoleon saw the charge he said it was an hour too soon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEspositoElting1999354,_Map_166_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEspositoElting1999354,_Map_166-134">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:French_cuirassiers_vs_Nassauers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2d/French_cuirassiers_vs_Nassauers.jpg/220px-French_cuirassiers_vs_Nassauers.jpg" width="220" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2d/French_cuirassiers_vs_Nassauers.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:French_cuirassiers_vs_Nassauers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>French <a href="/wiki/Cuirassier" title="Cuirassier">Cuirassiers</a></i>, by Louis Dumoulin</div></div></div>\n<p>Wellington\'s infantry responded by forming <a href="/wiki/Infantry_square" title="Infantry square">squares</a> (hollow box-formations four ranks deep). Squares were much smaller than usually depicted in paintings of the battle \xe2\x80\x93 a 500-man battalion square would have been no more than 60 feet (18&#160;m) in length on a side. Squares that stood their ground were deadly to cavalry, as cavalry could not engage with soldiers behind a hedge of bayonets, but were themselves vulnerable to fire from the squares. Horses would not charge a square, nor could they be outflanked, but they were vulnerable to artillery or infantry. Wellington ordered his artillery crews to take shelter within the squares as the cavalry approached, and to return to their guns and resume fire as they retreated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895444,_447_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895444,_447-138">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001273,_414_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001273,_414-139">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Witnesses in the British infantry recorded as many as 12&#160;assaults, though this probably includes successive waves of the same general attack; the number of general assaults was undoubtedly far fewer. Kellermann, recognising the futility of the attacks, tried to reserve the elite <i><a href="/wiki/Carabiniers-%C3%A0-Cheval" title="Carabiniers-\xc3\xa0-Cheval">carabinier</a></i> brigade from joining in, but eventually Ney spotted them and insisted on their involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001359_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001359-140">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A British eyewitness of the first French cavalry attack, an officer in the Foot Guards, recorded his impressions very lucidly and somewhat poetically:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>About four p.m., the enemy\'s artillery in front of us ceased firing all of a sudden, and we saw large masses of cavalry advance: not a man present who survived could have forgotten in after life the awful grandeur of that charge. You discovered at a distance what appeared to be an overwhelming, long moving line, which, ever advancing, glittered like a stormy wave of the sea when it catches the sunlight. On they came until they got near enough, whilst the very earth seemed to vibrate beneath the thundering tramp of the mounted host. One might suppose that nothing could have resisted the shock of this terrible moving mass. They were the famous cuirassiers, almost all old soldiers, who had distinguished themselves on most of the battlefields of Europe. In an almost incredibly short period they were within twenty yards of us, shouting <i>"Vive l\'Empereur!"</i> The word of command, "Prepare to receive cavalry", had been given, every man in the front ranks knelt, and a wall bristling with steel, held together by steady hands, presented itself to the infuriated cuirassiers.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Captain Rees Howell Gronow, Foot Guards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGronow1862&#39;&#39;The_Duke_of_Wellington_in_our_square&#39;&#39;_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGronow1862&#39;&#39;The_Duke_of_Wellington_in_our_square&#39;&#39;-141">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg/220px-Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg/330px-Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg/440px-Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg 2x" data-file-width="946" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Artillery_in_Battle_of_Waterloo_by_Jones.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The artillery officers had the range so accurately, that every shot and shell fell into the very centre of their masses" (Original inscription and drawing after <a href="/wiki/George_Jones_(painter)" title="George Jones (painter)">George Jones</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>In essence this type of massed cavalry attack relied almost entirely on psychological shock for effect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeller1992211,212_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeller1992211,212-142">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Close artillery support could disrupt infantry squares and allow cavalry to penetrate; at Waterloo, however, co-operation between the French cavalry and artillery was not impressive. The French artillery did not get close enough to the Anglo-allied infantry in sufficient numbers to be decisive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001252,361_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001252,361-143">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Artillery fire between charges did produce mounting casualties, but most of this fire was at relatively long range and was often indirect, at targets beyond the ridge. If infantry being attacked held firm in their square defensive formations, and were not panicked, cavalry on their own could do very little damage to them. The French cavalry attacks were repeatedly repelled by the steadfast infantry squares, the harrying fire of British artillery as the French cavalry recoiled down the slopes to regroup, and the decisive countercharges of Wellington\'s light cavalry regiments, the Dutch heavy cavalry brigade, and the remaining effectives of the Household Cavalry. At least one artillery officer disobeyed Wellington\'s order to seek shelter in the adjacent squares during the charges. <a href="/wiki/Cavali%C3%A9_Mercer" title="Cavali\xc3\xa9 Mercer">Captain Mercer</a>, who commanded <a href="/wiki/G_Parachute_Battery_(Mercer%27s_Troop)_Royal_Horse_Artillery" title="G Parachute Battery (Mercer&#39;s Troop) Royal Horse Artillery">\'G\' Troop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horse_Artillery" title="Royal Horse Artillery">Royal Horse Artillery</a>, thought the Brunswick troops on either side of him so shaky that he kept his battery of six nine-pounders in action against the cavalry throughout, to great effect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a313\xe2\x80\x93315_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a313\xe2\x80\x93315-144">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;x&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I thus allowed them to advance unmolested until the head of the column might have been about fifty or sixty yards from us, and then gave the word, "Fire!" The effect was terrible. Nearly the whole leading rank fell at once; and the round shot, penetrating the column carried confusion throughout its extent&#160;... the discharge of every gun was followed by a fall of men and horses like that of grass before the mower\'s scythe. </p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Captain <a href="/wiki/Cavali%C3%A9_Mercer" title="Cavali\xc3\xa9 Mercer">Cavali\xc3\xa9 Mercer</a>, RHA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a321_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a321-146">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg/260px-Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg" width="260" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg/390px-Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg/520px-Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2408" data-file-height="1510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charge_of_the_French_Cuirassiers_at_Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British square puts up dogged resistance against attacking French cavalry.</div></div></div>\n<p>For reasons that remain unclear, no attempt was made to <a href="/wiki/Touch_hole" title="Touch hole">spike</a> other allied guns while they were in French possession. In line with Wellington\'s orders, gunners were able to return to their pieces and fire into the French cavalry as they withdrew after each attack. After numerous costly but fruitless attacks on the Mont-Saint-Jean ridge, the French cavalry was spent.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;y&#93;</a></sup> Their casualties cannot easily be estimated. Senior French cavalry officers, in particular the generals, experienced heavy losses. Four divisional commanders were wounded, nine brigadiers wounded, and one killed \xe2\x80\x93 testament to their courage and their habit of leading from the front.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001359_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001359-140">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Illustratively, Houssaye reports that the <i>Grenadiers \xc3\xa0 Cheval</i> numbered 796 of all ranks on 15 June, but just 462 on 19 June, while the Empress Dragoons lost 416 of 816 over the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoussaye1900522_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoussaye1900522-148">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Overall Guyot\'s Guard heavy cavalry division lost 47% of its strength.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_French_infantry_attack">Second French infantry attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Second French infantry attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_(d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg/220px-Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg/330px-Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg/440px-Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_%28d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charge_des_lanciers_de_la_Garde_%C3%A0_Waterloo_(d%C3%A9tail_du_Panorama_de_Waterloo).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/2e_r%C3%A9giment_de_chevau-l%C3%A9gers_lanciers_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="2e r\xc3\xa9giment de chevau-l\xc3\xa9gers lanciers de la Garde Imp\xc3\xa9riale">2nd Guard Lancers</a> with the <i><a href="/wiki/Grenadiers_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Grenadiers \xc3\xa0 Cheval de la Garde Imp\xc3\xa9riale">Grenadiers \xc3\xa0 Cheval</a></i> in support<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;z&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Eventually it became obvious, even to Ney, that cavalry alone were achieving little. Belatedly, he organised a combined-arms attack, using Bachelu\'s division and Tissot\'s regiment of Foy\'s division from Reille\'s II Corps (about 6,500&#160;infantrymen) plus those French cavalry that remained in a fit state to fight. This assault was directed along much the same route as the previous heavy cavalry attacks (between Hougoumont and La Haye Sainte).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001361_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001361-150">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It was halted by a charge of the Household Brigade cavalry led by Uxbridge. The British cavalry were unable, however, to break the French infantry, and fell back with losses from musketry fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne189114,_38\xe2\x80\x9339_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne189114,_38\xe2\x80\x9339-151">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Uxbridge recorded that he tried to lead the Dutch Carabiniers, under Major-General <a href="/wiki/Albert_Dominicus_Trip_van_Zoudtlandt" title="Albert Dominicus Trip van Zoudtlandt">Trip</a>, to renew the attack and that they refused to follow him. Other members of the British cavalry staff also commented on this occurrence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1891pp._14\xe2\x80\x9315_and_letters_6,7_and_9_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1891pp._14\xe2\x80\x9315_and_letters_6,7_and_9-152">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> However, there is no support for this incident in Dutch or Belgian sources.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;aa&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Bachelu\'s and Tissot\'s men and their cavalry supports were being hard hit by fire from artillery and from Adam\'s infantry brigade, and they eventually fell back.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001361_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001361-150">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Although the French cavalry caused few direct casualties to Wellington\'s centre, artillery fire onto his infantry squares caused many. Wellington\'s cavalry, except for Sir John Vandeleur\'s and Sir Hussey Vivian\'s brigades on the far left, had all been committed to the fight, and had taken significant losses. The situation appeared so desperate that the Cumberland Hussars, the only Hanoverian cavalry regiment present, fled the field spreading alarm all the way to Brussels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895465_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895465-154">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;ab&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_capture_of_La_Haye_Sainte">French capture of La Haye Sainte</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: French capture of La Haye Sainte">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The storming of La Haye Sainte by Kn\xc3\xb6tel" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg/220px-Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg/330px-Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg/440px-Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Knotel_-_The_storming_of_La_Haye_Sainte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The storming of La Haye Sainte</i> by Kn\xc3\xb6tel</div></div></div>\n<p>At approximately the same time as Ney\'s combined-arms assault on the centre-right of Wellington\'s line, rallied elements of D\'Erlon\'s I Corps, spearheaded by the 13th <i>L\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa8re,</i> renewed the attack on La Haye Sainte and this time were successful, partly because the King\'s German Legion\'s ammunition ran out. However, the Germans had held the centre of the battlefield for almost the entire day, and this had stalled the French advance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimms201459\xe2\x80\x9360,_63\xe2\x80\x9364_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimms201459\xe2\x80\x9360,_63\xe2\x80\x9364-156">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeamish1995367_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeamish1995367-157">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With La Haye Sainte captured, Ney then moved skirmishers and <a href="/wiki/Horse_artillery" title="Horse artillery">horse artillery</a> up towards Wellington\'s centre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895483_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895483-158">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> French artillery began to pulverise the infantry squares at short range with <a href="/wiki/Canister_shot" title="Canister shot">canister</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895484_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895484-159">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The 30th and 73rd Regiments suffered such heavy losses that they had to combine to form a viable square.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006236_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006236-160">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The possession of La Haye Sainte by the French was a very dangerous incident. It uncovered the very centre of the Anglo-Allied army, and established the enemy within 60 yards of that centre. The French lost no time in taking advantage of this, by pushing forward infantry supported by guns, which enabled them to maintain a most destructive fire upon Alten\'s left and Kempt\'s right ...</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Captain James Shaw, <a href="/wiki/43rd_Foot" class="mw-redirect" title="43rd Foot">43rd Foot</a>, Chief of Staff 3rd Division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999134_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999134-161">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The success Napoleon needed to continue his offensive had occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006234_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006234-162">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Ney was on the verge of breaking the Allied centre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999134_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999134-161">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Along with this artillery fire a multitude of French <i>tirailleurs</i> occupied the dominant positions behind La Haye Sainte and poured an effective fire into the squares. The situation for the Anglo-allies was now so dire that the 33rd Regiment\'s colours and all of Halkett\'s brigade\'s colours were sent to the rear for safety, described by historian Alessandro Barbero as, "...&#160;a measure that was without precedent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006241_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006241-163">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Wellington, noticing the slackening of fire from La Haye Sainte, with his staff rode closer to it. French skirmishers appeared around the building and fired on the British command as it struggled to get away through the hedgerow along the road. Alten ordered a single battalion, the Fifth KGL to recapture the farm. Their Colonel Ompteda obeyed and chased off some French skirmishers until French cuirassiers fell on his open flank, killed him, destroyed his battalion and took its colour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006234_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006234-162">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A Dutch\xe2\x80\x93Belgian cavalry regiment ordered to charge, retreated from the field instead, fired on by their own infantry. Merlen\'s Light Cavalry Brigade charged the French artillery taking position near La Haye Sainte but were shot to pieces and the brigade fell apart. The Netherlands Cavalry Division, Wellington\'s last cavalry reserve behind the centre having lost half their strength was now useless and the French cavalry, despite its losses, were masters of the field compelling the allied infantry to remain in square. More and more French artillery was brought forward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006235\xe2\x80\x93236_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006235\xe2\x80\x93236-164">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A French battery advanced to within 300 yards of the 1/1st Nassau square causing heavy casualties. When the Nassauers attempted to attack the battery they were ridden down by a squadron of cuirassiers . Yet another battery deployed on the flank of Mercer\'s battery and shot up its horses and limbers and pushed Mercer back. Mercer later recalled, "The rapidity and precision of this fire was quite appalling. Every shot almost took effect, and I certainly expected we should all be annihilated. ... The saddle-bags, in many instances were torn from horses\' backs ... One shell I saw explode under the two finest wheel-horses in the troop down they dropped".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006235\xe2\x80\x93236_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006235\xe2\x80\x93236-164">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a325\xe2\x80\x93326_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a325\xe2\x80\x93326-165">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>French <i>tirailleurs</i> occupied the dominant positions, especially one on a knoll overlooking the square of the 27th. Unable to break square to drive off the French infantry because of the presence of French cavalry and artillery, the 27th had to remain in that formation and endure the fire of the <i>tirailleurs</i>. That fire nearly annihilated the 27th Foot, the Inniskillings, who lost two-thirds of their strength within that three or four hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006239_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006239-166">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The banks on the road side, the garden wall, the knoll and sandpit swarmed with skirmishers, who seemed determined to keep down our fire in front; those behind the artificial bank seemed more intent upon destroying the 27th, who at this time, it may literally be said, were lying dead in square; their loss after La Haye Sainte had fallen was awful, without the satisfaction of having scarcely fired a shot, and many of our troops in rear of the ridge were similarly situated.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Edward Cotton, 7th Hussars, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECotton1849106\xe2\x80\x93107_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECotton1849106\xe2\x80\x93107-167">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote><p>\nDuring this time many of Wellington\'s generals and aides were killed or wounded including Somerset, Canning, de Lancey, Alten and Cooke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006240_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006240-168">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The situation was now critical and Wellington, trapped in an infantry square and ignorant of events beyond it, was desperate for the arrival of help from the Prussians. He later wrote, </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The time they occupied in approaching seemed interminable. Both they and my watch seemed to have stuck fast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006242_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006242-169">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> \n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_the_Prussian_IV_Corps:_Plancenoit">Arrival of the Prussian IV Corps: Plancenoit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Arrival of the Prussian IV Corps: Plancenoit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign:_Ligny_through_Wavre_to_Waterloo#Prussians_take_possession_of_Wood_of_Paris" title="Waterloo Campaign: Ligny through Wavre to Waterloo">Prussians attack out of the Wood of Paris</a></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Night or the Prussians must come.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Wellington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006242_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006242-169">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg/260px-Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg" width="260" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg/390px-Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg/520px-Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1188" data-file-height="795" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prussian_Attack_Plancenoit_by_Adolf_Northern.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Prussian attack on <a href="/wiki/Plancenoit" title="Plancenoit">Plancenoit</a> painted by <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Northen" title="Adolph Northen">Adolph Northen</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first Prussian corps to arrive in strength was B\xc3\xbclow\'s IV Corps. B\xc3\xbclow\'s objective was Plancenoit, which the Prussians intended to use as a springboard into the rear of the French positions. Bl\xc3\xbccher intended to secure his right upon the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teaux_Frichermont" class="mw-redirect" title="Ch\xc3\xa2teaux Frichermont">Ch\xc3\xa2teaux Frichermont</a> using the Bois de Paris road.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999116_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999116-170">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Bl\xc3\xbccher and Wellington had been exchanging communications since 10:00 and had agreed to this advance on Frichermont if Wellington\'s centre was under attack.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er199995_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er199995-171">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;ac&#93;</a></sup> General B\xc3\xbclow noted that the way to Plancenoit lay open and that the time was 16:30.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999116_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999116-170">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At about this time, the 15th Brigade IV Corps was sent to link up with the Nassauers of Wellington\'s left flank in the Frichermont-<a href="/wiki/La_Haye,_Lasne" title="La Haye, Lasne">La Haie</a> area, with the brigade\'s horse artillery battery and additional brigade artillery deployed to its left in support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999117_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999117-173">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon sent Lobau\'s corps to stop the rest of B\xc3\xbclow\'s IV Corps proceeding to Plancenoit. The 15th Brigade threw Lobau\'s troops out of Frichermont with a determined bayonet charge, then proceeded up the Frichermont heights, battering French Chasseurs with 12-pounder artillery fire, and pushed on to Plancenoit. This sent Lobau\'s corps into retreat to the Plancenoit area, driving Lobau past the rear of the <i>Armee Du Nord\'s</i> right flank and directly threatening its only line of retreat. Hiller\'s 16th Brigade also pushed forward with six battalions against Plancenoit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="from the last citation lots of facts so a citation is needed (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon had dispatched all eight battalions of the Young Guard to reinforce Lobau, who was now seriously pressed. The Young Guard counter-attacked and, after very hard fighting, secured Plancenoit, but were themselves counter-attacked and driven out.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999122_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999122-174">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon sent two battalions of the Middle/Old Guard into Plancenoit and after ferocious bayonet fighting\xe2\x80\x94they did not deign to fire their muskets\xe2\x80\x94this force recaptured the village.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999122_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999122-174">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Zieten.27s_flank_march"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Zieten\'s_flank_march">Zieten\'s flank march</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Zieten&#039;s flank march">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg/310px-Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg" width="310" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg/465px-Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="605" data-file-height="612" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Waterloo_map.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Situation from 17:30 to 20:00</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the late afternoon, Zieten\'s I Corps had been arriving in greater strength in the area just north of La Haie. General M\xc3\xbcffling, Prussian liaison to Wellington, rode to meet I Corps.\n</p><p>Zieten had by this time brought up his 1st Brigade, but had become concerned at the sight of stragglers and casualties from the Nassau units on Wellington\'s left and from the Prussian 15th Brigade. These troops appeared to be withdrawing and Zieten, fearing that his own troops would be caught up in a general retreat, was starting to move away from Wellington\'s flank and towards the Prussian main body near Plancenoit. Zieten had also received a direct order from Bl\xc3\xbccher to support B\xc3\xbclow, which Zieten obeyed and marched to B\xc3\xbclow\'s aid.\n</p><p>M\xc3\xbcffling saw this movement away and persuaded Zieten to support Wellington\'s left flank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999125_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999125-175">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> M\xc3\xbcffling warned Zieten that "The battle is lost if the corps does not keep on the move and immediately support the English army".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002232_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002232-176">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Zieten resumed his march to support Wellington directly, and the arrival of his troops allowed Wellington to reinforce his crumbling centre by moving cavalry from his left.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999125_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999125-175">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French were expecting Grouchy to march to their support from Wavre, and when Zieten\'s I Corps appeared at Waterloo instead of Grouchy, "the shock of disillusionment shattered French morale" and "the sight of Zieten\'s arrival caused turmoil to rage in Napoleon\'s army".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002233_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002233-177">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> I Corps proceeded to attack the French troops before Papelotte and by 19:30 the French position was bent into a rough horseshoe shape. The ends of the line were now based on Hougoumont on the left, Plancenoit on the right, and the centre on La Haie.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999139_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999139-178">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Durutte had taken the positions of La Haie and Papelotte in a series of attacks,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999139_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999139-178">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> but now retreated behind Smohain without opposing the Prussian 24th Regiment as it retook both. The 24th advanced against the new French position, was repulsed, and returned to the attack supported by Silesian <i>Sch\xc3\xbctzen</i> (riflemen) and the F/1st <i>Landwehr</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999140_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999140-179">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The French initially fell back before the renewed assault, but now began seriously to contest ground, attempting to regain Smohain and hold on to the ridgeline and the last few houses of Papelotte.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999140_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999140-179">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 24th Regiment linked up with a Highlander battalion on its far right and along with the 13th <i>Landwehr</i> regiment and cavalry support threw the French out of these positions. Further attacks by the 13th <i>Landwehr</i> and the 15th Brigade drove the French from Frichermont.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999141_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999141-180">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Durutte\'s division, finding itself about to be charged by massed squadrons of Zieten\'s I Corps cavalry reserve, retreated from the battlefield. The soldiers of D\'Erlon\'s Corps alongside this attack on Durutte\'s division also broke and fled in panic, while to the west the French Middle Guard were assaulting Wellington\'s centre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002232\xe2\x80\x93233_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUffindellCorum2002232\xe2\x80\x93233-181">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesney1874187\xe2\x80\x93190_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesney1874187\xe2\x80\x93190-182">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The Prussian I Corps then advanced towards the Brussels road and the only line of retreat available to the French.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_of_the_Imperial_Guard">Attack of the Imperial Guard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Attack of the Imperial Guard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg/220px-Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg/330px-Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg/440px-Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1026" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crofts-Napoleon%27s_last_grand_attack_at_Waterloo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon addresses the <a href="/wiki/Old_Guard_(France)" title="Old Guard (France)">Old Guard</a> as it prepares to attack the Anglo-allied centre at Waterloo.</div></div></div>\n<p>Meanwhile, with Wellington\'s centre exposed by the fall of La Haye Sainte and the Plancenoit front temporarily stabilised, Napoleon committed his last reserve, the hitherto-undefeated Imperial Guard infantry. This attack, mounted at around 19:30, was intended to break through Wellington\'s centre and roll up his line away from the Prussians. Although it is one of the most celebrated passages of arms in military history, it had been unclear which units actually participated. It appears that it was mounted by five battalions of the Middle Guard,<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;ad&#93;</a></sup> and not by the grenadiers or chasseurs of the Old Guard. Three Old Guard battalions did move forward and formed the attack\'s second line, though they remained in reserve and did not directly assault the allied line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001391_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkin2001391-184">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;ae&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...&#160;I saw four regiments of the middle guard, conducted by the Emperor, arriving. With these troops, he wished to renew the attack, and penetrate the centre of the enemy. He ordered me to lead them on; generals, officers and soldiers all displayed the greatest intrepidity; but this body of troops was too weak to resist, for a long time, the forces opposed to it by the enemy, and it was soon necessary to renounce the hope which this attack had, for a few moments, inspired. </p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Marshal M. Ney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth181573,74_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth181573,74-186">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png/170px-Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png" width="170" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png/255px-Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png/340px-Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="654" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soldat-der-Alten-Garde.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Grenadier" title="Grenadier">Grenadier</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Guard_(France)" title="Old Guard (France)">Old Guard</a> in <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Le_Grenadier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Le Grenadier (page does not exist)">Le Grenadier</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Detaille" title="\xc3\x89douard Detaille">\xc3\x89douard Detaille</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Napoleon himself oversaw the initial deployment of the Middle and Old Guard. The Middle Guard formed in battalion squares, each about 550 men strong, with the 1st/3rd Grenadiers, led by Generals <a href="/wiki/Louis_Friant" title="Louis Friant">Friant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul-Jean-Baptiste_Poret_de_Morvan" title="Paul-Jean-Baptiste Poret de Morvan">Poret de Morvan</a>, on the right along the road, to their left and rear was General Harlet leading the square of the 4th Grenadiers, then the 1st/3rd Chasseurs under General <a href="/wiki/Claude-Etienne_Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Claude-Etienne Michel">Michel</a>, next the 2nd/3rd Chasseurs and finally the large single square of two battalions of 800 soldiers of the 4th Chasseurs led by General Henrion. Two batteries of Imperial Guard Horse Artillery accompanied them with sections of two guns between the squares. Each square was led by a general and Marshal Ney, mounted on his 5th horse of the day, led the advance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEField2013191\xe2\x80\x93192_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEField2013191\xe2\x80\x93192-187">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Behind them, in reserve, were the three battalions of the Old Guard, right to left 1st/2nd Grenadiers, 2nd/2nd Chasseurs and 1st/2nd Chasseurs. Napoleon left Ney to conduct the assault, however Ney led the Middle Guard on an oblique towards the Allied centre right instead of attacking straight up the centre. Napoleon sent Ney\'s senior ADC Colonel Crabb\xc3\xa9 to order Ney to adjust, but Crabb\xc3\xa9 was unable to get there in time.\n</p><p>Other troops rallied to support the advance of the Guard. On the left infantry from Reille\'s corps that was not engaged with Hougoumont and cavalry advanced. On the right all the now rallied elements of D\'\xc3\x89rlon\'s corps once again ascended the ridge and engaged the allied line. Of these, P\xc3\xa9got\'s brigade broke into skirmish order and moved north and west of La Haye Sainte and provided fire support to Ney, once again unhorsed, and Friant\'s 1st/3rd Grenadiers. The Guards first received fire from some Brunswick battalions, but the return fire of the grenadiers forced them to retire. Next, Colin Halket\'s brigade front line consisting of the 30th Foot and 73rd traded fire but they were driven back in confusion into the 33rd and 69th regiments, Halket was shot in the face and seriously wounded and the whole brigade retreated in a mob. Other allied troops began to give way as well. A counterattack by the Nassauers and the remains of Kielmansegge\'s brigade from the allied second line, led by the Prince of Orange, was also thrown back and the Prince of Orange was seriously wounded. General Harlet brought up the 4th Grenadiers and the allied centre was now in serious danger of breaking.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg/170px-General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg/255px-General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg/340px-General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2662" data-file-height="3346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_D_H_Chass%C3%A9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General <a href="/wiki/David_Hendrik_Chass%C3%A9" title="David Hendrik Chass\xc3\xa9">David Hendrik Chass\xc3\xa9</a></div></div></div>\n<p>It was at this moment that the timely arrival of the Dutch General <a href="/wiki/David_Hendrik_Chass%C3%A9" title="David Hendrik Chass\xc3\xa9">Chass\xc3\xa9</a> turned the tide in favour of the Anglo-allied army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEField2013196\xe2\x80\x93199_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEField2013196\xe2\x80\x93199-188">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>Chass\xc3\xa9\'s relatively fresh Dutch division was sent against them, led by a battery of Dutch horse-artillery commanded by Captain <a href="/wiki/Carel_Frederik_Krahmer_de_Bichin" title="Carel Frederik Krahmer de Bichin">Krahmer de Bichin</a>. The battery opened a destructive fire into the 1st/3rd Grenadiers\' flank.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> This still did not stop the Guard\'s advance, so Chass\xc3\xa9 ordered his first brigade (Colonel <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Detmers" title="Hendrik Detmers">Hendrik Detmers</a>) to charge the outnumbered French with the bayonet; the French grenadiers then faltered and broke.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The 4th Grenadiers, seeing their comrades retreat and having suffered heavy casualties themselves, now wheeled right about and retired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEField2013199_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEField2013199-191">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plas_Newydd_(Anglesey)_-_Waterloo_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg/220px-Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg/330px-Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg/440px-Plas_Newydd_%28Anglesey%29_-_Waterloo_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plas_Newydd_(Anglesey)_-_Waterloo_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British 10th Hussars of Vivian\'s Brigade (red shakos \xe2\x80\x93 blue uniforms) attacking mixed French troops, including a square of Guard grenadiers (left, middle distance) in the final stages of the battle.</div></div></div>\n<p>To the left of the 4th Grenadiers were the two squares of the 1st/ and 2nd/3rd Chasseurs who angled further to the west and had suffered more from artillery fire than the grenadiers. But as their advance mounted the ridge they found it apparently abandoned and covered with dead. Suddenly 1,500 British Foot Guards under <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_Maitland" title="Peregrine Maitland">Maitland</a> who had been lying down to protect themselves from the French artillery rose and devastated them with point-blank volleys. The chasseurs deployed to answer the fire, but some 300 fell from the first volley, including Colonel Mallet and General Michel, and both battalion commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEField2013200_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEField2013200-192">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> A bayonet charge by the Foot Guards then broke the leaderless squares, which fell back onto the following column. The 4th Chasseurs battalion, 800 strong, now came up onto the exposed battalions of British Foot Guards, who lost all cohesion and dashed back up the slope as a disorganized crowd with the chasseurs in pursuit. At the crest the chasseurs came upon the battery that had caused severe casualties on the 1st and 2nd/3rd Chasseurs. They opened fire and swept away the gunners. The left flank of their square now came under fire from a heavy formation of British skirmishers, which the chasseurs drove back. But the skirmishers were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/52nd_(Oxfordshire)_Regiment_of_Foot" title="52nd (Oxfordshire) Regiment of Foot">52nd Light Infantry</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/John_Colborne" class="mw-redirect" title="John Colborne">John Colborne</a>, which wheeled in line onto the chasseurs\' flank and poured a devastating fire into them. The chasseurs returned a very sharp fire which killed or wounded some 150 men of the 52nd.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEField2013203_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEField2013203-193">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The 52nd then charged,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesney1874214\xe2\x80\x93215_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesney1874214\xe2\x80\x93215-194">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParry190070_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParry190070-195">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> and under this onslaught, the chasseurs broke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParry190070_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParry190070-195">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The last of the Guard retreated headlong. A ripple of panic passed through the French lines as the astounding news spread: "<i>La Garde recule. Sauve qui peut</i>!" ("The Guard is retreating. Every man for himself!") Wellington now stood up in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_(horse)" title="Copenhagen (horse)">Copenhagen\'s</a> stirrups and waved his hat in the air to signal a general advance. His army rushed forward from the lines and threw themselves upon the retreating French. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesney1874192,_225_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesney1874192,_225-196">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895553\xe2\x80\x93559_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895553\xe2\x80\x93559-197">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The surviving Imperial Guard rallied on their three reserve battalions (some sources say four) just south of La Haye Sainte for a <a href="/wiki/Last_stand" title="Last stand">last stand</a>. A charge from <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Adam" title="Frederick Adam">Adam\'s</a> Brigade and the Hanoverian <i>Landwehr</i> Osnabr\xc3\xbcck Battalion, plus Vivian\'s and Vandeleur\'s relatively fresh cavalry brigades to their right, threw them into confusion. Those left in semi-cohesive units retreated towards <i>La Belle Alliance</i>.
