It was during this retreat that some of the Guards were invited to surrender, eliciting the famous, if apocryphal,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;af&#93;</a></sup> retort "<i>La Garde meurt, elle ne se rend pas!</i>" ("The Guard dies, it does not surrender!")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2011_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2011-199">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;ag&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prussian_capture_of_Plancenoit"><span id="Capture_of_Plancenoit"></span>Prussian capture of Plancenoit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prussian capture of Plancenoit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg/330px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg/440px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1113" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Elsholtz_Erst%C3%BCrmung_von_Planchenois.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The storming of Plancenoit by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Elsholtz" title="Ludwig Elsholtz">Ludwig Elsholtz</a></div></div></div>\n<p>At about the same time, the Prussian 5th, 14th, and 16th Brigades were starting to push through Plancenoit, in the third assault of the day. The church was by now on fire, while its graveyard\xe2\x80\x94the French centre of resistance\xe2\x80\x94had corpses strewn about "as if by a whirlwind". Five Guard battalions were deployed in support of the Young Guard, virtually all of which was now committed to the defence, along with remnants of Lobau\'s corps. The key to the Plancenoit position proved to be the Chantelet woods to the south. Pirch\'s II Corps had arrived with two brigades and reinforced the attack of IV Corps, advancing through the woods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145-201">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 25th Regiment\'s musketeer battalions threw the 1/2e Grenadiers (Old Guard) out of the Chantelet woods, outflanking Plancenoit and forcing a retreat. The Old Guard retreated in good order until they met the mass of troops retreating in panic, and became part of that rout. The Prussian IV Corps advanced beyond Plancenoit to find masses of French retreating in disorder from British pursuit. The Prussians were unable to fire for fear of hitting Wellington\'s units. This was the fifth and final time that Plancenoit changed hands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145-201">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>French forces not retreating with the Guard were surrounded in their positions and eliminated, neither side asking for nor offering quarter. The French Young Guard Division reported 96&#160;per cent casualties, and two-thirds of Lobau\'s Corps ceased to exist.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg/170px-Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg/255px-Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg/340px-Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1941" data-file-height="2419" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuirass_holed_by_a_canonball_at_Waterloo_Antoine_Fauveau_18Juin_1815.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Carabiniers-%C3%A0-Cheval" title="Carabiniers-\xc3\xa0-Cheval">Carabinier-\xc3\xa0-Cheval</a></i> cuirass holed by a cannonball at Waterloo, belonging to Antoine Fauveau (<a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Arm%C3%A9e" title="Mus\xc3\xa9e de l&#39;Arm\xc3\xa9e">Mus\xc3\xa9e de l\'Arm\xc3\xa9e</a>)</div></div></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Despite their great courage and stamina, the French Guards fighting in the village began to show signs of wavering. The church was already on fire with columns of red flame coming out of the windows, aisles and doors. In the village itself\xe2\x80\x94still the scene of bitter house-to-house fighting\xe2\x80\x94everything was burning, adding to the confusion. However, once Major von Witzleben\'s manoeuvre was accomplished and the French Guards saw their flank and rear threatened, they began to withdraw. The Guard Chasseurs under General <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Germain_Pelet-Clozeau" title="Jean-Jacques Germain Pelet-Clozeau">Pelet</a> formed the rearguard. The remnants of the Guard left in a great rush, leaving large masses of artillery, equipment and ammunition wagons in the wake of their retreat. The evacuation of Plancenoit led to the loss of the position that was to be used to cover the withdrawal of the French Army to Charleroi. The Guard fell back from Plancenoit in the direction of Maison du Roi and Caillou. Unlike other parts of the battlefield, there were no cries of "Sauve qui peut!" here. Instead, the cry "Sauvons nos aigles!" ("Let\'s save our eagles!") could be heard.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Official History of the 25th Regiment, 4 Corps, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145-201">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_disintegration">French disintegration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: French disintegration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png/260px-Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png" width="260" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png/390px-Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png/520px-Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png 2x" data-file-width="1026" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dernier_carre_de_la_Garde_-_gen_Hill.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rowland_Hill,_1st_Viscount_Hill" title="Rowland Hill, 1st Viscount Hill">Lord Hill</a> invites the last remnants of the French Imperial Guard to surrender, painted by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Alexander_Hillingford" title="Robert Alexander Hillingford">Robert Alexander Hillingford</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The French right, left, and centre had all now failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145_201-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999144\xe2\x80\x93145-201">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The last cohesive French force consisted of two battalions of the Old Guard stationed around <i>La Belle Alliance</i>; they had been so placed to act as a final reserve and to protect Napoleon in the event of a French retreat. He hoped to rally the French army behind them,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKincaid2006435_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKincaid2006435-203">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> but as retreat turned into rout, they too were forced to withdraw, one on either side of <i>La Belle Alliance</i>, in square as protection against Coalition cavalry. Until persuaded that the battle was lost and he should leave, Napoleon commanded the square to the left of the inn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreasy1877&#91;httpwwwstandinsefifteen15ahtm_Chapter_XV&#93;_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreasy1877[httpwwwstandinsefifteen15ahtm_Chapter_XV]-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComte_d&#39;Erlon1815_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComte_d&#39;Erlon1815-204">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Adam\'s Brigade charged and forced back this square,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParry190070_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParry190070-195">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999149_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999149-205">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> while the Prussians engaged the other.\n</p><p>As dusk fell, both squares withdrew in relatively good order, but the French artillery and everything else fell into the hands of the Prussian and Anglo-allied armies. The retreating Guards were surrounded by thousands of fleeing, broken French troops. Coalition cavalry harried the fugitives until about 23:00, with Gneisenau pursuing them as far as Genappe before ordering a halt. There, Napoleon\'s abandoned carriage was captured, still containing <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Annotated_Prince" class="extiw" title="s:The Annotated Prince">an annotated copy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccol\xc3\xb2 Machiavelli">Machiavelli</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince" title="The Prince">The Prince</a></i>, and diamonds left behind in the rush to escape. These diamonds became part of King Friedrich Wilhelm of Prussia\'s crown jewels; one Major Keller of the F/15th received the <a href="/wiki/Pour_le_M%C3%A9rite" title="Pour le M\xc3\xa9rite">Pour le M\xc3\xa9rite</a> with oak leaves for the feat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999151_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999151-206">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> By this time 78&#160;guns and 2,000&#160;prisoners had also been taken, including more generals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999150_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999150-207">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There remained to us still four squares of the Old Guard to protect the retreat. These brave grenadiers, the choice of the army, forced successively to retire, yielded ground foot by foot, till, overwhelmed by numbers, they were almost entirely annihilated. From that moment, a retrograde movement was declared, and the army formed nothing but a confused mass. There was not, however, a total rout, nor the cry of <i>sauve qui peut</i>, as has been calumniously stated in the bulletin.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Marshal M. Ney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth181574_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth181574-208">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the middle of the position occupied by the French army, and exactly upon the height, is a farm (sic), called <i>La Belle Alliance</i>. The march of all the Prussian columns was directed towards this farm, which was visible from every side. It was there that Napoleon was during the battle; it was thence that he gave his orders, that he flattered himself with the hopes of victory; and it was there that his ruin was decided. There, too, it was that, by happy chance, Field Marshal Bl\xc3\xbccher and Lord Wellington met in the dark, and mutually saluted each other as victors.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>General Gneisenau.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth181523_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth181523-209">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Other sources agree that the meeting of the commanders took place near <i>La Belle Alliance</i>, with this occurring at around 21:00.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies2012244_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies2012244-210">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorrigan2006327_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorrigan2006327-211">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Campaign#Invasion_of_France_and_the_occupation_of_Paris_(18_June_\xe2\x80\x93_7_July)" title="Waterloo Campaign">Waterloo Campaign \xc2\xa7&#160;Invasion of France and the occupation of Paris (18 June \xe2\x80\x93 7 July)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1815)" title="Treaty of Paris (1815)">Treaty of Paris (1815)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/220px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/330px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/440px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The morning after the battle of Waterloo", by <a href="/wiki/John_Heaviside_Clark" title="John Heaviside Clark">John Heaviside Clark</a>, 1816</div></div></div>\n<p>Waterloo cost Wellington around 15,000 dead or wounded and Bl\xc3\xbccher some 7,000 (810 of which were suffered by just one unit: the 18th Regiment, which served in B\xc3\xbclow\'s 15th Brigade, had fought at both Frichermont and Plancenoit, and won 33 <a href="/wiki/Iron_Cross" title="Iron Cross">Iron Crosses</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMantle2000_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMantle2000-212">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon\'s losses were 24,000 to 26,000 killed or wounded and included 6,000 to 7,000 captured with an additional 15,000 deserting subsequent to the battle and over the following days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006312_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006312-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>22&#160;June. This morning I went to visit the field of battle, which is a little beyond the village of Waterloo, on the plateau of Mont-Saint-Jean; but on arrival there the sight was too horrible to behold. I felt sick in the stomach and was obliged to return. The multitude of carcasses, the heaps of wounded men with mangled limbs unable to move, and perishing from not having their wounds dressed or from hunger, as the Anglo-allies were, of course, obliged to take their surgeons and waggons with them, formed a spectacle I shall never forget. The wounded, both of the Anglo-allies and the French, remain in an equally deplorable state.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Major W. E. Frye.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrye2004&#39;&#39;June_22&#39;&#39;_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrye2004&#39;&#39;June_22&#39;&#39;-213">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg/220px-Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg/330px-Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg/440px-Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1827" data-file-height="2368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Invasion_of_France_in_1815.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Invasion of France by the Seventh Coalition armies in 1815</div></div></div>\n<p>At 10:30 on 19 June General Grouchy, still following his orders, defeated General Thielemann at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wavre" title="Battle of Wavre">Wavre</a> and withdrew in good order\xe2\x80\x94though at the cost of 33,000 French troops that never reached the Waterloo battlefield. Wellington sent <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Wellingon%27s_Waterloo_dispatch_to_Lord_Bathurst,_19_June_1815" class="extiw" title="s:Wellingon&#39;s Waterloo dispatch to Lord Bathurst, 19 June 1815">his official dispatch</a> describing the battle to England on 19 June 1815; it arrived in London on 21 June 1815 and was published as a <i><a href="/wiki/London_Gazette" class="mw-redirect" title="London Gazette">London Gazette Extraordinary</a></i> on 22 June.<sup id="cite_ref-Gazette,_17028_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gazette,_17028-214">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Wellington, Bl\xc3\xbccher and other Coalition forces advanced upon Paris.\n</p><p>Napoleon announced his <a href="/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon_(1815)" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdication of Napoleon (1815)">second abdication</a> on 24 June 1815. In the final skirmish of the Napoleonic Wars, Marshal <a href="/wiki/Davout" class="mw-redirect" title="Davout">Davout</a>, Napoleon\'s minister of war, was defeated by Bl\xc3\xbccher at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issy" title="Battle of Issy">Issy</a> on 3 July 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1907_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1907-215">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Allegedly, Napoleon tried to escape to North America, but the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> was blockading French ports to forestall such a move. He finally surrendered to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(Royal_Navy)" title="Captain (Royal Navy)">Captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lewis_Maitland_(Rear_Admiral)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Lewis Maitland (Rear Admiral)">Frederick Maitland</a> of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Ship" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Ship">HMS</a> <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Bellerophon_(1786)" title="HMS Bellerophon (1786)">Bellerophon</a></i> on 15 July. There was a campaign against French fortresses that still held out; <a href="/wiki/Longwy" title="Longwy">Longwy</a> capitulated on 13 September 1815, the last to do so. <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XVIII">Louis XVIII</a> was restored to the throne of France and Napoleon was exiled to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a>, where he died in 1821. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1815)" title="Treaty of Paris (1815)">Treaty of Paris</a> was signed on 20 November 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999274\xe2\x80\x93276,320_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofschr\xc3\xb6er1999274\xe2\x80\x93276,320-216">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Royal Highness, \xe2\x80\x93 Exposed to the factions which divide my country, and to the enmity of the great Powers of Europe, I have terminated my political career; and I come, like <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, to throw myself upon the hospitality (<i>m\'asseoir sur le foyer</i>) of the British people. I claim from your Royal Highness the protections of the laws, and throw myself upon the most powerful, the most constant, and the most generous of my enemies.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Napoleon. (letter of surrender to the <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">Prince Regent</a>; translation).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth181557_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth181557-217">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg/260px-David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg" width="260" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg/390px-David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg/520px-David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3108" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Wilkie_Chelsea_Pensioners_Reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Wilkie_(artist)" title="David Wilkie (artist)">Sir David Wilkie</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chelsea_Pensioners_reading_the_Waterloo_Dispatch" class="mw-redirect" title="The Chelsea Pensioners reading the Waterloo Dispatch">The Chelsea Pensioners reading the Waterloo Dispatch</a></i>, 1822</div></div></div>\n<p>Maitland\'s <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">1st Foot Guards</a>, who had defeated the Chasseurs of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guard_(Napoleon_I)" title="Imperial Guard (Napoleon I)">Guard</a>, were thought to have defeated the Grenadiers, although they had only faced Chasseurs of the newly raised Middle Guard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006264_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2006264-218">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> They were nevertheless awarded the title of <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">Grenadier Guards</a> in recognition of their feat and adopted bearskins in the style of the Grenadiers. Britain\'s Household Cavalry likewise adopted the cuirass in 1821 in recognition of their success against their armoured French counterparts. The effectiveness of the lance was noted by all participants and this weapon subsequently became more widespread throughout Europe; the British converted their first light cavalry regiment to lancers in 1816, their uniforms, of Polish origin, were based on those of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_1st_Light_Cavalry_Regiment_of_the_Imperial_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish 1st Light Cavalry Regiment of the Imperial Guard">Imperial Guard lancers</a>.\n</p><p>Teeth of tens of thousands of dead soldiers were removed by surviving troops, locals or even scavengers who had travelled there from Britain, then used for making denture replacements in Britain and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_importance">Historical importance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Historical importance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Waterloo proved a decisive battle in more than one sense. Every generation in Europe up to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> looked back at Waterloo as the turning point that dictated the course of subsequent world history, seeing it in retrospect as the event that ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a>, an era characterised by relative peace, material prosperity and technological progress.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\nThe battle definitively ended the series of wars that had convulsed Europe, and involved many other regions of the world, since the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of the early 1790s. It also ended the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a> and the political and military career of Napoleon Bonaparte, one of the greatest commanders and statesmen in history.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;ah&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There followed almost four decades of international peace in Europe. No further major conflict occurred until the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of 1853-1856. Changes to the configuration of European states, as refashioned after Waterloo, included the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Alliance" title="Holy Alliance">Holy Alliance</a> of reactionary governments intent on repressing revolutionary and democratic ideas, and the reshaping of the former <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> into a <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> increasingly marked by the political dominance of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>. The bicentenary of Waterloo prompted renewed attention in the geopolitical and economic legacy of the battle and in the century of relative transatlantic peace which followed.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;ai&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Views_on_the_reasons_for_Napoleon.27s_defeat"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_the_reasons_for_Napoleon\'s_defeat">Views on the reasons for Napoleon\'s defeat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Views on the reasons for Napoleon&#039;s defeat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>General <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Henri_Jomini" title="Antoine-Henri Jomini">Antoine-Henri</a>, Baron Jomini, one of the leading military writers on the Napoleonic art of war, had a number of very cogent explanations of the reasons behind Napoleon\'s defeat at Waterloo.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;aj&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>In my opinion, four principal causes led to this disaster:\n</p><p>\nThe first, and most influential, was the arrival, skilfully combined, of Bl\xc3\xbccher, and the false movement that favoured this arrival;<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;ak&#93;</a></sup> the second, was the admirable firmness of the British infantry, joined to the sang-froid and aplomb of its chiefs; the third, was the horrible weather, that had softened the ground, and rendered the offensive movements so toilsome, and retarded till one o\'clock the attack that should have been made in the morning; the fourth, was the inconceivable formation of the first corps, in masses very much too deep for the first grand attack.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Antoine-Henri Jomini.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJomini1864223,224_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJomini1864223,224-230">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The Prussian soldier, historian, and theorist <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a>, who as a young colonel had served as chief-of-staff to Thielmann\'s Prussian III Corps during the Waterloo campaign, expressed the following opinion:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>\nBonaparte and the authors who support him have always attempted to portray the great catastrophes that befell him as the result of chance. They seek to make their readers believe that through his great wisdom and extraordinary energy the whole project had already moved forward with the greatest confidence, that complete success was but a hair\'s breadth away, when treachery, accident, or even fate, as they sometimes call it, ruined everything. He and his supporters do not want to admit that huge mistakes, sheer recklessness, and, above all, overreaching ambition that exceeded all realistic possibilities, were the true causes.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Carl von Clausewitz.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClausewitzFeldzug_von_1815_(&#39;&#39;Campaign_of_1815&#39;&#39;)&#39;&#39;&#91;httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815five1-9htmCh3_Chapter_3&#93;&#39;&#39;_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClausewitzFeldzug_von_1815_(&#39;&#39;Campaign_of_1815&#39;&#39;)&#39;&#39;[httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815five1-9htmCh3_Chapter_3]&#39;&#39;-231">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Wellington himself wrote in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Wellingon%27s_Waterloo_dispatch_to_Lord_Bathurst,_19_June_1815" class="extiw" title="s:Wellingon&#39;s Waterloo dispatch to Lord Bathurst, 19 June 1815">his official dispatch</a> back to London:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I should not do justice to my own feelings, or to Marshal Bl\xc3\xbccher and the Prussian army, if I did not attribute the successful result of this arduous day to the cordial and timely assistance I received from them. The operation of General B\xc3\xbclow upon the enemy\'s flank was a most decisive one; and, even if I had not found myself in a situation to make the attack which produced the final result, it would have forced the enemy to retire if his attacks should have failed, and would have prevented him from taking advantage of them if they should unfortunately have succeeded".<sup id="cite_ref-Gazette,_17028_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gazette,_17028-214">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Despite their differences on other matters, discussed at length in Carl von Clausewitz\'s study of the <i>Campaign of 1815</i> and <i>Wellington\'s famous 1842 essay</i> in reply to it, the Prussian Clausewitz agreed with Wellington on this assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClausewitzWellington2010&#39;&#39;&#91;httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815five1-9htm_Campaign_of_1815&#93;&#39;&#39;_and_&#39;&#39;&#91;httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815sixhtm_Wellington&#39;s_famous_1842_essay&#93;&#39;&#39;_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClausewitzWellington2010&#39;&#39;[httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815five1-9htm_Campaign_of_1815]&#39;&#39;_and_&#39;&#39;[httpwwwclausewitzcomreadings1815sixhtm_Wellington&#39;s_famous_1842_essay]&#39;&#39;-232">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, Clausewitz viewed the battle prior to the Prussian intervention more as a mutually exhausting stalemate than as an impending French victory, with the advantage, if any, leaning towards Wellington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClausewitzWellington2010144\xe2\x80\x93145_(Chapter_41_of_Clausewitz&#39;s_&#39;&#39;Feldzug&#39;&#39;)_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClausewitzWellington2010144\xe2\x80\x93145_(Chapter_41_of_Clausewitz&#39;s_&#39;&#39;Feldzug&#39;&#39;)-233">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An alternative view is that towards the end of the battle Wellington\'s Anglo-allied army faced imminent defeat without Prussian help. For example, Parkinson (2000) writes: "Neither army beat Napoleon alone. But whatever the part played by Prussian troops in the actual moment when the Imperial Guard was repulsed, it is difficult to see how Wellington could have staved off defeat, when his centre had been almost shattered, his reserves were almost all committed, the French right remained unmolested and the Imperial Guard intact. \xe2\x80\xa6. Bl\xc3\xbccher may not have been totally responsible for victory over Napoleon, but he deserved full credit for preventing a British defeat".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkinson2000240\xe2\x80\x93241_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkinson2000240\xe2\x80\x93241-234">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Steele (2014) writes: "Bl\xc3\xbccher\'s arrival not only diverted vital reinforcements, but also forced Napoleon to accelerate his effort against Wellington. The tide of battle had been turned by the hard-driving Bl\xc3\xbccher. As his Prussians pushed in Napoleon\'s flank. Wellington was able to shift to the offensive".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteele2014178_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteele2014178-235">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battlefield_today">Battlefield today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Battlefield today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Waterloo_Battlefield_locations" title="List of Waterloo Battlefield locations">List of Waterloo Battlefield locations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG/220px-0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG/330px-0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG/440px-0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_%281%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2256" data-file-height="1672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:0_Braine-l%27Alleud_051012_(1).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lion%27s_Mound" title="Lion&#39;s Mound">Lion\'s Mound</a> at Waterloo</div></div></div>\n<p>Some portions of the terrain on the battlefield have been altered from their 1815 appearance. Tourism began the day after the battle, with Captain Mercer noting that on 19 June "a carriage drove on the ground from Brussels, the inmates of which, alighting, proceeded to examine the field".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a345_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMercer1870a345-236">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In 1820, the Netherlands\' King <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_the_Netherlands" title="William I of the Netherlands">William I</a> ordered the construction of a monument. The <a href="/wiki/Lion%27s_Hillock" class="mw-redirect" title="Lion&#39;s Hillock">Lion\'s Hillock</a>, a giant mound, was constructed here using 300,000 cubic metres (390,000&#160;cu&#160;yd) of earth taken from the ridge at the centre of the British line, effectively removing the southern bank of Wellington\'s sunken road.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Every one is aware that the variously inclined undulations of the plains, where the engagement between Napoleon and Wellington took place, are no longer what they were on 18&#160;June 1815. By taking from this mournful field the wherewithal to make a monument to it, its real relief has been taken away, and history, disconcerted, no longer finds her bearings there. It has been disfigured for the sake of glorifying it. Wellington, when he beheld Waterloo once more, two years later, exclaimed, "They have altered my field of battle!" Where the great pyramid of earth, surmounted by the lion, rises to-day, there was a hillock which descended in an easy slope towards the Nivelles road, but which was almost an escarpment on the side of the highway to Genappe. The elevation of this escarpment can still be measured by the height of the two knolls of the two great sepulchres which enclose the road from Genappe to Brussels: one, the English tomb, is on the left; the other, the German tomb, is on the right. There is no French tomb. The whole of that plain is a sepulchre for France.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables" title="Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables">Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHugo1862Chapter_VII:_Napoleon_in_a_Good_Humor_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHugo1862Chapter_VII:_Napoleon_in_a_Good_Humor-237">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The alleged remark by Wellington about the alteration of the battlefield as described by Hugo was never documented, however.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other terrain features and notable landmarks on the field have remained virtually unchanged since the battle. These include the rolling farmland to the east of the Brussels\xe2\x80\x93Charleroi Road as well as the buildings at Hougoumont, La Haye Sainte, and La Belle Alliance.\n</p><p>Apart from the Lion Mound, there are several more conventional but noteworthy monuments throughout the battlefield. A cluster of monuments at the Brussels\xe2\x80\x93Charleroi and Braine L\'Alleud\xe2\x80\x93Ohain crossroads marks the mass graves of British, Dutch, Hanoverian and <a href="/wiki/King%27s_German_Legion" title="King&#39;s German Legion">King\'s German Legion</a> troops. A monument to the French dead, entitled <i>L\'Aigle bless\xc3\xa9</i> ("The Wounded Eagle"), marks the location where it is believed one of the Imperial Guard units formed a square during the closing moments of the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoorebeeke20076\xe2\x80\x9321_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoorebeeke20076\xe2\x80\x9321-239">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A monument to the Prussian dead is located in the village of <a href="/wiki/Plancenoit" title="Plancenoit">Plancenoit</a> on the site where one of their artillery batteries took position. The <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Philibert_Duhesme" title="Guillaume Philibert Duhesme">Duhesme</a> mausoleum is one among the few graves of the fallen. It is located at the side of Saint Martin\'s Church in Ways, a hamlet in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Genappe" title="Genappe">Genappe</a>. Seventeen fallen officers are buried in the crypt of the <a href="/wiki/Brussels_Cemetery#The_British_Waterloo_Campaign_Monument" title="Brussels Cemetery">British Monument</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brussels_Cemetery" title="Brussels Cemetery">Brussels Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Evere" title="Evere">Evere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoorebeeke20076\xe2\x80\x9321_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoorebeeke20076\xe2\x80\x9321-239">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The remains of a 23-year-old soldier named Friederich Brandt were discovered in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaton2015_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaton2015-240">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> He was a slightly hunchbacked infantryman, 1.60 metres (5.2&#160;ft) tall, and was hit in the chest by a French bullet. His rifle, coins, and position on the battlefield identified him as an Hanoverian fighting in the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_German_Legion" title="King&#39;s German Legion">King\'s German Legion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeel2012_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeel2012-241">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coin_controversy">Coin controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Coin controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As part of the bicentennial celebration of the battle, in 2015 <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> minted a 2 <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">Euro</a> coin depicting the Lion monument over a map of the field of battle. France officially protested against this issue of coins, while the Belgian government noted that the French mint sells souvenir medals at Waterloo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorfs2015_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorfs2015-242">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> After 180,000 coins were minted but not released, the issue was melted. Instead, Belgium issued an identical commemorative coin in the non-standard value of 2\xc2\xbd Euros. Legally only valid within the issuing country (but unlikely to circulate) it was minted in brass, packaged, and sold by the Belgian mint for 6 Euros. A 10 Euro coin, showing Wellington, Bl\xc3\xbccher, their troops and the silhouette of Napoleon, was also available in silver for 42 Euros.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKottasova2015_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKottasova2015-243">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleonic_Eagle.svg" class="image"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Napoleonic_Eagle.svg/30px-Napoleonic_Eagle.svg.png" width="30" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Napoleonic_Eagle.svg/45px-Napoleonic_Eagle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Napoleonic_Eagle.svg/60px-Napoleonic_Eagle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="321" /></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Napoleonic_Wars" title="Portal:Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lord_Uxbridge%27s_leg" title="Lord Uxbridge&#39;s leg">Lord Uxbridge\'s leg</a> was shattered by a <a href="/wiki/Grape-shot" class="mw-redirect" title="Grape-shot">grape-shot</a> at the Battle of Waterloo and removed by a surgeon. The artificial leg used by Uxbridge for the rest of his life was donated to a Waterloo Museum after his death. There is also a leg on display at his house, Plas Newydd, on Anglesey.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Order_of_battle_of_the_Waterloo_Campaign" title="Order of battle of the Waterloo Campaign">Order of battle of the Waterloo Campaign</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> (1803\xe2\x80\x931815): involved Napoleon Bonaparte\'s French Empire against the United Kingdom and various combinations of other European powers in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Coalition">Third</a> to <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Coalition" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh Coalition">Seventh</a> coalitions.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Napoleonic_era" title="Timeline of the Napoleonic era">Timeline of the Napoleonic era</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Waterloo_in_popular_culture" title="Waterloo in popular culture">Waterloo in popular culture</a>: describes the cultural impact of the battle.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Medal" title="Waterloo Medal">Waterloo Medal</a> awarded to those soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> who fought at the battle.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo_reenactment" title="Battle of Waterloo reenactment">Battle of Waterloo reenactment</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Waterloo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Captain <a href="/wiki/Cavali%C3%A9_Mercer" title="Cavali\xc3\xa9 Mercer">Cavali\xc3\xa9 Mercer</a> <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horse_Artillery" title="Royal Horse Artillery">RHA</a>, thought the Brunswickers "...perfect children. None of the privates, perhaps were over eighteen years of age" (<a href="#CITEREFMercer1891">Mercer 1891</a>, p.&#160;218).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 13 June, the commandant at <a href="/wiki/Ath" title="Ath">Ath</a> requested powder and cartridges as members of a Hanoverian reserve regiment there had never yet fired a shot (<a href="#CITEREFLongford1971">Longford 1971</a>, p.&#160;486).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The straight-line distance from Halle to Braine-l\'Alleud, Wellington\'s far right flank is nearly the same as the straight-line distance from Wavre to <a href="/wiki/Frichermont" class="mw-redirect" title="Frichermont">Frichermont</a>, Wellington\'s far left flank, around 8 miles (13&#160;km).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The hour at which Waterloo began, though there were 150,000&#160;actors in the great tragedy, was long a matter of dispute. The Duke of Wellington puts it at 10:00. General Alava says half-past eleven, Napoleon and Drouet say noon, and Ney 13:00. Lord Hill may be credited with having settled this minute question of fact. He took two watches with him into the fight, one a stop-watch, and he marked with it the sound of the first shot fired, and this evidence is now accepted as proving that the first flash of red flame which marked the opening of the world-shaking tragedy of Waterloo took place at exactly ten minutes to twelve" (<a href="#CITEREFFitchett2006">Fitchett 2006</a>, Chapter: King-making Waterloo). "...watches had to be set by solar time, this meant that it was rare for two watches to agree... For example, on 9 June, ... the French I Corps had been at Lille, while the IV Corps was at Metz. Assuming an officer had set his watch at noon and then meticulously wound it twice a day but not reset it during the approach march, by the time the two corps reached the vicinity of Waterloo, the I Corps officer\'s watch would have read 12:40 p.m. at a time when the IV Corps officer\'s read 11:20 a.m., and it was noon at Waterloo. This is an extreme example, and unlikely to have actually been the case, but it demonstrates the problem quite well" (<a href="#CITEREFNofi1998">Nofi 1998</a>, p.&#160;182).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That is, the 1st battalion of the 2nd Regiment. Among Prussian regiments, "F/12th" denoted the fusilier battalion of the 12th Regiment.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seeing the flames, Wellington sent a note to the house\'s commander stating that he must hold his position whatever the cost (<a href="#CITEREFBarbero2005">Barbero 2005</a>, p.&#160;298).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Lord Hill may be credited with having settled this minute question of fact. He took two watches with him into the fight, one a stop-watch, and he marked with it the sound of the first shot fired&#160;... At ten minutes to twelve the first heavy gun rang sullenly from the French ridge" (<a href="#CITEREFFitchett2006">Fitchett 2006</a>, Chapter: King-making Waterloo).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The entire 1st brigade of the 2nd Dutch division, that had been on the forward slope during the night, withdrew to a position behind the ridge between the regiments of Kempt and Pack around 12:00 (<a href="#CITEREFBasWommersom1909">Bas &amp; Wommersom 1909</a>, pp.&#160;332\xe2\x80\x93333).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Website of current Dutch historian Marco Bijl: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.8militia.net/">8militia.net</a>;<a href="#CITEREFEenens1879">Eenens 1879</a>, pp.&#160;14\xe2\x80\x9330, 131\xe2\x80\x93198; De Jongh, W.A.: Veldtocht van den Jare 1815, Historisch verhaal; in De Nieuwe Militaire Spectator (Nijmegen 1866), pp. 13\xe2\x80\x9327.(This is the original account of Colonel de Jongh, commander of the Dutch 8th Militia. It can be downloaded at the site of Marco Bijl above.); L\xc3\xb6ben Sels, Ernst van Bijdragen tot de krijgsgeschiedenis van Napoleon Bonaparte / door E. van L\xc3\xb6ben Sels Part 4; Veldtogten van 1814 in Frankrijk, en van 1815 in de Nederlanden (Battles). 1842. \'s-Gravenhage&#160;: de Erven Doorman, pp. 601\xe2\x80\x93682; Allebrandi, Sebastian. Herinneringen uit mijne tienjarige militaire loopbaan. 1835. Amsterdam&#160;: Van Kesteren, pp. 21\xe2\x80\x9330;(Allebrandi was a soldier in the Dutch 7th Militia his account is therefore important).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">De Bas reprints colonel Van Zuylen\'s \'History of the 2nd division\'. Van Zuylen van Nijevelt was the chief of staff of the 2nd division and located right behind the van Bijlandt brigade during the whole day (<a href="#CITEREFBasWommersom1909">Bas &amp; Wommersom 1909</a>, pp.&#160;134\xe2\x80\x93136(vol.2)). He wrote a 32 page report, right after the battle. This report forms the basis of most of the other literature mentioned here: see <a href="#CITEREFBasWommersom1909">Bas &amp; Wommersom 1909</a>, pp.&#160;289\xe2\x80\x93352(vol.3). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=V5wLAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA61">Google Books</a>; Boulger has an English translation of the report (<a href="#CITEREFBoulger1901">Boulger 1901</a>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The brigade\'s losses were very heavy: one French volley at point blank range decimated the 7th and 8th Militia, who had most of their officers killed or wounded, the brigade commander Bijlandt being one of the wounded who had to be evacuated. The two battalions lost their command structure in one stroke. The total of casualties for the whole brigade for the day was around 800 killed and wounded (<a href="#CITEREFHamilton-Williams1993">Hamilton-Williams 1993</a>, pp.&#160;310\xe2\x80\x93311).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Van Zuylen report; he refers to himself as "the chief-of-staff" (<a href="#CITEREFBasWommersom1909">Bas &amp; Wommersom 1909</a>, pp.&#160;338\xe2\x80\x93339(vol. 3)).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some of the retreating troops panicked and fled. This was not to be wondered at in the circumstances. The British troops of the 1/95th battalion, also under great pressure from the French, did the same at the time. This flight did not involve the whole of the Dutch battalions, as has been asserted by some historians. Van Zuylen rallied 400 men, according to his estimate, who were ready to join the counterattack and even captured two French fanions (<a href="#CITEREFBasWommersom1909">Bas &amp; Wommersom 1909</a>, pp.&#160;338\xe2\x80\x93341(vol. 3); <a href="#CITEREFHamilton-Williams1993">Hamilton-Williams 1993</a>, pp.&#160;293\xe2\x80\x93295).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPawly2001">Pawly 2001</a>, pp.&#160;37\xe2\x80\x9343; The following letters are used: the accounts of General Kempt, Calvert of the 32nd infantry, Cruikshank of the 79th, Winchester &amp; Hope of the 92nd, Evans (Ponsonby Cavalry brigade) and Clark Kennedy of the Royal Dragoons (<a href="#CITEREFGlover2004">Glover 2004</a>, p.&#160;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>). These are the only letters that actually state some details about the Dutch and Belgian troops.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Royal Horse Guards (2 squadrons) were in reserve for the Household Brigade (9 or 10 squadrons strong) but the Union Brigade (9 squadrons) had no reserve (<i>Letter 5</i>, <a href="#CITEREFSiborne1891">Siborne 1891</a>, pp.&#160;7\xe2\x80\x9310; <i>Letter 16</i> <a href="#CITEREFGlover2004">Glover 2004</a>). The total may have been 18&#160;squadrons as there is an uncertainty in the sources as to whether the King\'s Dragoon Guards fielded three or four squadrons. Uxbridge implies 4 squadrons (<i>Letter 5</i> <a href="#CITEREFSiborne1891">Siborne 1891</a>, pp.&#160;7\xe2\x80\x9310), however, Capt. Naylor of the King\'s implies 3 when he states he commanded the centre squadron of the regiment (<i>Letter 21</i>, <a href="#CITEREFSiborne1891">Siborne 1891</a>, pp.&#160;46\xe2\x80\x9347).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An episode famously used later by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables" title="Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables">Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The sunken lane acted as a trap, funnelling the flight of the French cavalry to their own right and away from the British cavalry. Some of the cuirassiers then found themselves hemmed in by the steep sides of the sunken lane, with a confused mass of their own infantry in front of them, the 95th Rifles firing at them from the north side of the lane, and Somerset\'s heavy cavalry still pressing them from behind.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiborne1895410\xe2\x80\x93411_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiborne1895410\xe2\x80\x93411-96">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The novelty of fighting armoured foes impressed the British cavalrymen, as was recorded by the commander of the Household Brigade.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The tale was related, in old age, by a <a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Major" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergeant Major">Sergeant Major</a> Dickinson of the Scots Greys, the last British survivor of the charge (<a href="#CITEREFLow1911">Low 1911</a>, pp.&#160;137, 143).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Losses are ultimately from the official returns taken the day after the battle: Household Brigade, initial strength 1,319, killed&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;95, wounded&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;248, missing&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;250, totals&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;593, horses lost&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;672.<br />\nUnion Brigade, initial strength 1,332, killed&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;264, wounded&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;310, missing&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;38, totals&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;612, horses lost&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;631 (<a href="#CITEREFSmith1998">Smith 1998</a>, p.&#160;544).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This view appears to have arisen from a comment by Captain Clark-Kennedy of the 1st Dragoons \'Royals\', in a letter in <a href="/wiki/H._T._Siborne" class="mw-redirect" title="H. T. Siborne">H. T. Siborne\'s</a> book, he makes an estimate of around 900&#160;men actually in line within the Union Brigade before its first charge (<a href="#CITEREFSiborne1891">Siborne 1891</a>, <i>Letter 35</i>, p. 69). Clark-Kennedy does not, however, explain how his estimate was arrived at. The shortfall of 432&#160;men (the equivalent of a whole regiment) from the paper strength of the brigade is large. However, another officer of the brigade, John Mills of the 2nd Dragoons, says that the effective strength of the brigade did not "exceed 1,200" (<a href="#CITEREFGlover2007">Glover 2007</a>, p.&#160;59).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/William_Siborne" title="William Siborne">William Siborne</a> was in possession of a number of eyewitness accounts from generals, such as Uxbridge, down to cavalry cornets and infantry ensigns. This makes his history particularly useful (though only from the British and KGL perspective); some of these eyewitness letters were later published by his son, a British Major General (<a href="/wiki/H._T._Siborne" class="mw-redirect" title="H. T. Siborne">H. T. Siborne</a>). Parts of William Siborne\'s account was, and is, highly controversial. The very negative light shed on the conduct of the Dutch\xe2\x80\x93Belgian troops during the battle by Siborne, which it should be said was a reasonably accurate reflection the opinions of his British informants, prompted a semi-official rebuttal by Dutch historian captain <a href="/wiki/Willem_Jan_Knoop" title="Willem Jan Knoop">Willem Jan Knoop</a> in his <i>"Beschouwingen over Siborne\'s Geschiedenis van den oorlog van 1815 in Frankrijk en de Nederlanden" en wederlegging van de in dat werk voorkomende beschuldigingen tegen het Nederlandsche leger</i>. Breda 1846; 2nd printing 1847. Knoop based his rebuttal on the official Dutch after-battle reports, drawn up within days of the battle, not on twenty-year-old recollections of veterans, like Siborne. Siborne rejected the rebuttal.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barbero points out that in April the minister informed Wellington that cavalry regiments could allow themselves no more than 360 horses. The text of this memorandum from Torrens to Wellington Barbero refers to is available in Hamilton-Williams, p.75.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Losses are ultimately from the official returns taken the day after the battle: Household Brigade, initial strength 1,319, killed&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;95, wounded&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;248, missing&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;250, totals&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;593, horses lost&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;672.\nUnion Brigade, initial strength 1,332, killed&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;264, wounded&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;310, missing&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;38, totals&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;612, horses lost&#160;\xe2\x80\x93&#160;631 (<a href="#CITEREFSmith1998">Smith 1998</a>, p.&#160;544).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a cavalry unit an "effective" was an unwounded trooper mounted on a sound horse. The military term "effective" describes a soldier, piece of equipment (e.g. a tank or aircraft) or military unit capable of fighting or carrying out its intended purpose.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This qualification may have been self-serving on Mercer\'s part. Wellington himself sought refuge in the "shaky" Brunswick squares at the time and observed what he interpreted as acts of cowardice by British artillerymen, who "...&#160;ran off the field entirely, taking with them limbers, ammunition, and everything&#160;..." as he wrote in a letter of 21 December 1815 to the Master-General of the Ordnance, Lord Mulgrave. The incident even justified the denial of pensions to members of the Artillery Corps in his view. So, where Mercer claimed heroism, Wellington saw the opposite. See for the full text of Wellington\'s letter, and an attempted rebuttal <cite id="CITEREFDuncan1879" class="citation">Duncan, F. (1879), "Appendix A", <i>History of the Royal Regiment of Artillery</i>, pp.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/historyofroyalre02duncuoft#page/444/mode/2up">444</a>\xe2\x80\x93464</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Appendix+A&amp;rft.btitle=History+of+the+Royal+Regiment+of+Artillery&amp;rft.pages=444-464&amp;rft.date=1879&amp;rft.aulast=Duncan&amp;rft.aufirst=F.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABattle+of+Waterloo" class="Z3988"></span><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style> \xe2\x80\x94 The letter was originally published in <i>WSD</i>, vol. XIV (1858 ed.), pp. 618\xe2\x80\x93620</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cavalrymen were not allowed to dismount without orders, so individual initiative in spiking a cannon would have been impossible for any ranker. Each British cannon had a number of headless nails for spiking stored in a box on the gun carriage, so the French would have had the means to disable the guns readily available, had they known (<a href="#CITEREFWeller1992">Weller 1992</a>, p.&#160;114).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A number of different mounts could have been ridden by Napoleon at Waterloo: Ali, Creb\xc3\xa8re, D\xc3\xa9sir\xc3\xa9e, Jaffa, Marie and Tauris (<a href="#CITEREFSummerville2007">Summerville 2007</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=q_cXSrh0C_IC&amp;pg=PA315">p. 315</a>) Lozier states it was D\xc3\xa9sir\xc3\xa9e (<a href="#CITEREFLozier2010">Lozier 2010</a>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the contrary, many contradicted this British account vehemently. See e.g.<a href="#CITEREFEenens1879">Eenens 1879</a>, pp.&#160;131\xe2\x80\x93198. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=sxdJAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PT23">Google Books;</a> <cite id="CITEREFKnoop1847" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Willem_Jan_Knoop" title="Willem Jan Knoop">Knoop, W.J.</a> (1847) [1846], "Beschouwingen over Siborne\'s Geschiedenis van den oorlog van 1815 in Frankrijk en de Nederlanden", <i>en wederlegging van de in dat werk voorkomende beschuldigingen tegen het Nederlandsche leger</i> (2nd ed.), Breda</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Beschouwingen+over+Siborne%27s+Geschiedenis+van+den+oorlog+van+1815+in+Frankrijk+en+de+Nederlanden&amp;rft.btitle=en+wederlegging+van+de+in+dat+werk+voorkomende+beschuldigingen+tegen+het+Nederlandsche+leger&amp;rft.place=Breda&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.date=1847&amp;rft.aulast=Knoop&amp;rft.aufirst=W.J.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABattle+of+Waterloo" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/>; <cite id="CITEREFCraan1817" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Willem_Benjamin_Craan" title="Willem Benjamin Craan">Craan, W.B.</a> (1817), <i>An historical account of the battle of Waterloo</i>, translated by Gore, A., pp.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.co.uk/books?id=jEsBAAAAQAAJ&amp;pg=RA1-PA30">30</a>\xe2\x80\x9331</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=An+historical+account+of+the+battle+of+Waterloo&amp;rft.pages=30-31&amp;rft.date=1817&amp;rft.aulast=Craan&amp;rft.aufirst=W.B.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABattle+of+Waterloo" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/> \xe2\x80\x94 written in 1816 on the basis of eyewitness accounts does not mention the incident).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The commander of the Cumberland Hussars, who was later court-martialled and cashiered, claimed that as his troopers (all well-to-do young Hanoverians) owned their own horses he could not order them to remain on the field. Following the battle the regiment was broken up and the troopers assigned duties they, no doubt, considered ignominious. Four were posted to Captain Mercer\'s horse artillery troop, where he found them "amazingly sulky and snappish with every one".(<a href="#CITEREFMercer1870b">Mercer 1870b</a>, p.&#160;62) </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-172">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chesney states that Wellington and the Prussians remained in contact and that it was agreed that B\xc3\xbclow followed by Pirch would take the poorer road to "Froidmont" (Frichermont), while Zieten would take the longer northern, but better made, road via Ohain (<a href="#CITEREFChesney1874">Chesney 1874</a>, pp.&#160;173\xe2\x80\x93178).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Two chasseur battalions of the 4th Chasseurs were merged into one on the day of the battle, so while five Imperial Guard formations went forward, they may have comprised six battalions (<a href="#CITEREFBarbero2005">Barbero 2005</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>). Similarly, Lewis, 2013, pp. 188 \xe2\x80\x93 190.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (December 2016)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-185"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-185">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The attacking battalions were 1st/3rd and 4th Grenadiers and 1st/3rd, 2nd/3rd and 4th Chasseurs of the Middle Guard; those remaining in reserve were the 2nd/2nd Grenadiers, 2nd/1st and 2nd/2nd Chasseurs of the Old Guard (<a href="#CITEREFAdkin2001">Adkin 2001</a>, p.&#160;392).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-198">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"\'The Guard dies, but it does not surrender!\' is another of these fictitious historical sayings. General Cambronne, to whom it is attributed, never uttered. Victor Hugo, in Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables, has restored the true text. It is composed of a single word [<i>Merde!</i>]".(<a href="#CITEREFMasson1869">Masson 1869</a>)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The reply is commonly attributed to General <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Cambronne" title="Pierre Cambronne">Pierre Cambronne</a>, originating from an attribution by the journalist Balison de Rougemont in <i>Journal General</i> published on 24 June 1815,(<a href="#CITEREFShapiro2006">Shapiro 2006</a>, p.&#160;128) although Cambronne claimed he replied "<i>Merde!</i>" (<a href="#CITEREFBoller1989">Boller 1989</a>, p.&#160;12) However, according to letters in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> in June 1932, Cambronne was already a prisoner of Colonel <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Halkett" title="Hugh Halkett">Hugh Halkett</a>, so the retort, if ever given, or in whatever form it took, may have come from <a href="/wiki/Claude-Etienne_Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Claude-Etienne Michel">General Michel</a> instead.<a href="#CITEREFWhite2011">White 2011</a>, and <a href="#CITEREFParry1900">Parry 1900</a>, p.&#160;70 </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Through the finality of Napoleon\'s defeat, "met his/her Waterloo" has entered the English lexicon as a phrase to describe someone\'s circumstances when they have met with absolute and final defeat.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Napoleon\'s last escapade was important politically because it "compelled all the powers at Vienna to bury their remaining differences in order to achieve a peace which would enshrine the principles of the balance of power".(<a href="#CITEREFKennedy1987">Kennedy 1987</a>, p.&#160;37) "No international disturbance comparable in magnitude...has ever been followed by such a protracted period of peace". (<a href="#CITEREFPalmer1956">Palmer 1956</a>, p.&#160;420) Recovering, after Waterloo, from six decades of abnormal obstacles to transatlantic commerce (from the Seven Years War onwards), increasingly industrialized Europe and North America, by 1914, accounted for over 90% of global coal, iron and steel production and 76% of international trade.(<a href="#CITEREFPaxton1985">Paxton 1985</a>, p.&#160;2) </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-228"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-228">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jomini was Swiss, but was an officer, eventually a general, in the French army and had served on the staff of Marshal Ney. He later served in the Russian Army.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-229">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This "false movement" was the detachment of Grouchy\'s force in pursuit of the Prussians: Napoleon had overestimated the extent of his victory at Ligny and underestimated the resilience of the Prussians. He also seems to have discounted the presence of B\xc3\xbclow\'s substantial corps, which had not been in action at Ligny. Had Napoleon retained Grouchy\'s 30,000 men as a guard for his right flank, it is likely that these troops could have held off the Prussians and allowed the rest of Napoleon\'s army to attack Wellington\'s army unmolested.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;
